The Waterwheel Argument Against Indivisible Parts
Al-Iji presents the first scenario of the waterwheel to prove divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first scenario of the waterwheel uses the necessity of simultaneous fast and slow motions to argue that the body must be divisible.

Core Idea
Indivisible parts would force the millstone to disintegrate – an absurdity.

This lesson introduces the first of six scenarios presented by al-Iji to disprove atomism. By examining the fast and slow motions of the millstone's circles, he demonstrates that assuming indivisible parts would require the millstone to fall apart – a logical impossibility that undermines the atomist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the structure: statement of objective, enumeration, then the scenario.
Pay attention to the description of lines and rings.
2
Identify the two circles
The scenario distinguishes between the circumferential circle (ṭawqīya) and the polar circle (quṭbīya). Understand why their motions differ in speed.
3
Follow the chain of reasoning
Trace the argument: if the circumference moves but the polar stops, the millstone would disjoin. The illustration with lines from center to circumference shows this breakdown into rings.
The rings are composed of indivisible parts (ajzā' lā tatajazzā).
4
Consider the iron millstone example
Even if the millstone were made of iron, it would stick when still but disjoin when moving, showing the argument is about logical necessity, not physical strength.
5
Understand the conclusion
The mind judges this disjunction impossible, and God did not create such wonders to support atomism. The argument refutes atomism by showing an absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : الجسم البسيط واحد قابل للقسمة والبسيطة ) وهو الأمر الأول ، ( أو أقل لزم التجزؤ ) وهو الأمر الثاني . ( وذلك ) أي تلازم السريعة والبطيئة حاصل ( في صور ست : ( الأولى : الدائرة الطوقية من الرحى مع الدائرة القطبية (منها فإن حركة الأولى سريعة لطول مسافتها وحركة الثانية بطيئة لقصر مسافتها وهما متلازمتان (إذ لو تحركت الطوقية ( مثلا ) ووقفت القطبية لزم التفكك وانقسام الرحى إلى دوائر متعددة ( بحسب أجزائها) وإنما يتضح ذلك بإخراج خطوط متلاصقة من مركز الرحى إلى الطوق العظيم منها في جميع الجهات فإن تلك الخطوط تكون مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وتتركب من أجزاء تلك الخطوط أطواق متداخلة متفاوتة في الكبر والصغر والطوق العظيم منها مركب من أطراف هذه الخطوط، فإذا تحرك هذا الطوق ولم يتحرك الطوق الذي يلاصقه فقد انفك أحدهما الآخر، وكذا إذا تحرك الطوق الثاني، ولم يتحرك الثالث، وهكذا إلى الطوق الذي هو أصغرها فلزوم تفكك الرحى عند تحركها على مثال دوائر محيطة بعضها ببعض، ( ولو كانت ( الرحى ) من حديد أو ما هو أشد منه ثم التصاقها عند الوقوف بحيث لا يمكن أن يتفكك منها جزء بأبلغ السعي وذلك ) الذي ذكرناه من تفكك الرحى حال تحركها والتصاقها حال سكونها . ( وإن كان مما لا يمتنع في قدرة الله تعالى فالعقل حازم بعدمه كسائر العاديات ومعلوم ) لكل عاقل ( أن الله تعالى لم يخلق في الرحى كل هذه الغرائب و العجائب ليثبت مذهبكم .
قوله : ( فالعقل جازم إلخ ) أصحاب الجزء لا يمنعون الجزم بعدمه بل يقولون إنه مستبعد عادة وإذا ساق البرهان إلى تركيب الجسم من الأجزاء فلزوم المستبعدات لا يضره، كما قال الحكماء إن البرهان يتعلق إلى اتصال الجسم في نفسه فلزوم انعدام البحر بشق البعوضة ووجود البحرين الأخيرين المستبعد لا يضره أن ثبوت سكون بين كل حركتين يستلزم وقوف الجبل في الجو لممانعة الخردلة المستبعد ولا ضير في ذلك.
قوله : ( لزم التفكك وانقسام الرحى هاهنا منع مبني على قولهم أن محور الكرة لم يكن متحركاً حين ما يتحرك تلك الكرة على ذلك المحور مع أنه لم يلزم التفكك حينئذ أصلاً فعلى هذا لم لا يجوز أن يتحرك الرحى ولا يتحرك قطبه أصلا ويتحرك الدائرة القطبية منه تارة وتقف أخرى فترى حركتها أبطأ من حركة الدائرة الطوقية؟ ويكون ذلك بواسطة استعدادات شرائط مختلفة ومع ذلك لم يلزم تفكك الرحى أصلاً وهذا ليس بأبعد من القول باستمرار حركة الدحرجة زماناً على نقطة واحدة كما مر وكذا الكلام في سائر الصور الست، فتأمل. وقوله : ولو كانت الرحى هذا وصل متعلق بقوله : لزم التفكك وقوله : ثم التصادق مرفوع عطفاً على التفكك المذكور. قوله : كل من هذه الغرائب والعجائب ومن الغرائب هو ما أعطي كل من أجزاء الرحى
The First Observatory - The Fifth Objective: The simple body is one and divisible. And the slow (motion) is the first matter, or less necessitates division which is the second matter. That is, the simultaneity of the fast and slow occurs in six forms: First: The circumferential circle of the millstone (al-rahā) with the polar circle of it. For the motion of the first is fast due to the length of its distance, and the motion of the second is slow due to the shortness of its distance, and they are simultaneous. For if the circumferential moved and the polar stopped, necessary would be the disjoining and the splitting of the millstone into multiple circles according to its parts. And that becomes clear by extracting contiguous lines from the center of the millstone to its great circumference in all directions. For those lines are composed of indivisible parts, and from the parts of those lines are composed interlocking rings varying in size, and the great ring is composed of the ends of these lines. So if this ring moved and the ring adjacent to it did not move, then one of them would be separated from the other. Similarly, if the second ring moved and the third did not, and so on to the smallest ring. Thus the necessary disjoining of the millstone when it moves is on the example of circles surrounding one another. And if the millstone were of iron or what is harder than it, then its sticking together when stopping such that not a part can be disjoined from it with the utmost effort – that which we have mentioned: the disjoining of the millstone while moving and its sticking together while stationary. And if this is not impossible in the power of God, the mind judges it impossible like other customary things. And it is known to every rational person that God did not create all these wonders and marvels in the millstone to prove your doctrine. 
His saying: (the mind judges it impossible etc.) The proponents of the indivisible part do not deny the judgment of its impossibility, but they say it is customarily improbable, and when the proof leads to the composition of the body from parts, the necessity of improbable matters does not harm it, as the philosophers said that the proof relates to the continuity of the body in itself, so the necessity of the annihilation of the sea by the splitting of a mosquito and the existence of the two latter seas, which is improbable, does not harm it; that the establishment of rest between every two motions necessitates the suspension of the mountain in the air due to the resistance of a mustard seed, which is improbable, and there is no harm in that. 
His saying: (necessary would be the disjoining and splitting of the millstone here) is a denial based on their statement that the axis of the sphere was not moving when that sphere moves on that axis, while no disjoining occurs at that time at all. So on this basis, why is it not permissible that the millstone moves and its pole does not move at all, and the polar circle from it moves sometimes and stops other times, so you see its motion slower than the motion of the circumferential circle? And that is through different preparatory conditions, and yet no disjoining of the millstone occurs at all. This is not more improbable than the statement of the continuation of rolling motion for a time on a single point, as has passed. And likewise the discussion in all six forms, so reflect. And his saying: and if the millstone were... This is a conjunction related to his saying: necessary disjoining. And his saying: then the adhesion is nominative, coordinated with the mentioned disjoining. His saying: all these wonders and marvels, and among the wonders is what is given to each of the parts of the millstone.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a proof against atomism (the doctrine of indivisible parts). It begins by stating the objective: the simple body is one and divisible. It then presents the first of six scenarios that demonstrate the necessity of simultaneity (talāzum) between fast and slow motions, a consequence that would be impossible if the body were composed of indivisible parts. The scenario uses a millstone (raḥā) with a circumferential circle (ṭawqīya) and a polar circle (quṭbīya). The argument shows that if the two circles moved at different speeds but were attached, assuming indivisible parts would lead to the millstone disintegrating—an absurdity. The text then notes that the mind judges this impossibility as certain, but the atomists respond that such improbabilities do not refute a valid proof. The passage includes two objections: first, that the atomists accept the judgment of impossibility but consider it merely improbable, not logically impossible; second, that the necessity of disjoining can be denied if the axis is stationary. The commentator explains these objections and concludes with a remark about the wonders God could create. This lesson covers the full first scenario and its immediate refutations.

Key Terms
أجزاء لا تتجزأ
تلازم
تفكك
رحى
دائرة طوقية
دائرة قطبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that the millstone must actually disjoin when moving if it were made of indivisible parts.
Correction: The argument is a logical reductio: assuming indivisible parts forces an absurd consequence (disintegration), which shows that the assumption is false. It does not claim that the millstone actually disjoins in reality.

Checkpoint
What does the first scenario of the waterwheel aim to demonstrate?
A) That the millstone is made of iron
B) That fast and slow motions cannot be simultaneous
C) That assuming indivisible parts leads to absurd consequences like the millstone disintegrating
D) That God created wonders in the millstone

Key Takeaway
The millstone scenario demonstrates that the assumption of indivisible parts leads to a logical impossibility, proving that the body must be infinitely divisible.

Method of Learning
intermediate

Objections to the Waterwheel Argument and Responses
Al-Iji presents and refutes atomist objections to the waterwheel argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atomist objections to the waterwheel argument and how al-Iji's commentator defends the proof.

Core Idea
Atomists admit the improbability but claim it does not invalidate a logical proof.

After presenting the millstone scenario, al-Iji anticipates objections from proponents of indivisible parts. This lesson examines those objections: first, that the mind's judgment of impossibility is merely customary; second, that disjoining can be avoided if the axis is stationary. The responses highlight the nature of rational proof and the immunity of logical arguments from empirical improbabilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
The atomists argue that the mind's judgment of impossibility is only customary, not absolute. They allow that unlikely consequences do not harm a valid proof.
Compare with the philosophers' examples: sea annihilated by a mosquito, mountain suspended by a mustard seed.
2
Understand the atomist stance
They do not deny the improbability but maintain that if a proof leads to composition from parts, such improbabilities are irrelevant.
3
Examine the second objection
The objector denies necessary disjoining by pointing out that the axis may not move with the sphere. If the polar circle can move independently, no disjoining occurs.
This references the idea of a fixed axis in a rolling sphere.
4
Consider the commentator's rebuttal
The commentator notes that such special conditions (like a stationary axis) are not more improbable than the atomists' own claims about rolling motion on a point.
The argument stands because it relies on logical necessity, not empirical scenarios.
5
Reflect on the overall dialectic
The exchange shows that atomism cannot escape logical absurdity by appealing to custom or special cases. The proof remains valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : الجسم البسيط واحد قابل للقسمة والبسيطة ) وهو الأمر الأول ، ( أو أقل لزم التجزؤ ) وهو الأمر الثاني . ( وذلك ) أي تلازم السريعة والبطيئة حاصل ( في صور ست : ( الأولى : الدائرة الطوقية من الرحى مع الدائرة القطبية (منها فإن حركة الأولى سريعة لطول مسافتها وحركة الثانية بطيئة لقصر مسافتها وهما متلازمتان (إذ لو تحركت الطوقية ( مثلا ) ووقفت القطبية لزم التفكك وانقسام الرحى إلى دوائر متعددة ( بحسب أجزائها) وإنما يتضح ذلك بإخراج خطوط متلاصقة من مركز الرحى إلى الطوق العظيم منها في جميع الجهات فإن تلك الخطوط تكون مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ وتتركب من أجزاء تلك الخطوط أطواق متداخلة متفاوتة في الكبر والصغر والطوق العظيم منها مركب من أطراف هذه الخطوط، فإذا تحرك هذا الطوق ولم يتحرك الطوق الذي يلاصقه فقد انفك أحدهما الآخر، وكذا إذا تحرك الطوق الثاني، ولم يتحرك الثالث، وهكذا إلى الطوق الذي هو أصغرها فلزوم تفكك الرحى عند تحركها على مثال دوائر محيطة بعضها ببعض، ( ولو كانت ( الرحى ) من حديد أو ما هو أشد منه ثم التصاقها عند الوقوف بحيث لا يمكن أن يتفكك منها جزء بأبلغ السعي وذلك ) الذي ذكرناه من تفكك الرحى حال تحركها والتصاقها حال سكونها . ( وإن كان مما لا يمتنع في قدرة الله تعالى فالعقل حازم بعدمه كسائر العاديات ومعلوم ) لكل عاقل ( أن الله تعالى لم يخلق في الرحى كل هذه الغرائب و العجائب ليثبت مذهبكم .

That is, the simultaneity of the fast and slow occurs in six forms: First: The circumferential circle of the millstone (al-rahā) with the polar circle of it. So if this ring moved and the ring adjacent to it did not move, then one of them would be separated from the other. Thus the necessary disjoining of the millstone when it moves is on the example of circles surrounding one another. And if the millstone were of iron or what is harder than it, then its sticking together when stopping such that not a part can be disjoined from it with the utmost effort – that which we have mentioned: the disjoining of the millstone while moving and its sticking together while stationary. And if this is not impossible in the power of God, the mind judges it impossible like other customary things. And it is known to every rational person that God did not create all these wonders and marvels in the millstone to prove your doctrine. 
His saying: (the mind judges it impossible etc.) The proponents of the indivisible part do not deny the judgment of its impossibility, but they say it is customarily improbable, and when the proof leads to the composition of the body from parts, the necessity of improbable matters does not harm it, as the philosophers said that the proof relates to the continuity of the body in itself, so the necessity of the annihilation of the sea by the splitting of a mosquito and the existence of the two latter seas, which is improbable, does not harm it; that the establishment of rest between every two motions necessitates the suspension of the mountain in the air due to the resistance of a mustard seed, which is improbable, and there is no harm in that.

Commentary
This passage is a proof against atomism (the doctrine of indivisible parts). It begins by stating the objective: the simple body is one and divisible. It then presents the first of six scenarios that demonstrate the necessity of simultaneity (talāzum) between fast and slow motions, a consequence that would be impossible if the body were composed of indivisible parts. The scenario uses a millstone (raḥā) with a circumferential circle (ṭawqīya) and a polar circle (quṭbīya). The argument shows that if the two circles moved at different speeds but were attached, assuming indivisible parts would lead to the millstone disintegrating—an absurdity. The text then notes that the mind judges this impossibility as certain, but the atomists respond that such improbabilities do not refute a valid proof. The passage includes two objections: first, that the atomists accept the judgment of impossibility but consider it merely improbable, not logically impossible; second, that the necessity of disjoining can be denied if the axis is stationary. The commentator explains these objections and concludes with a remark about the wonders God could create. This lesson covers the full first scenario and its immediate refutations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The atomists reject the waterwheel argument outright by denying the possibility of disjoining.
Correction: The atomists actually accept the judgment of impossibility but argue that it does not refute a logical proof; they propose alternative scenarios to avoid the absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first objection in this passage?
A) The millstone cannot be made of iron
B) The mind's judgment of impossibility is absolute and cannot be ignored
C) The atomists accept the impossibility but consider it merely improbable, not enough to refute a proof
D) The axis must move with the sphere

Key Takeaway
Atomists concede the absurdity of the millstone scenario but argue that improbable consequences do not invalidate a rational proof, a position the commentator refutes by pointing out the special pleading involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because its implications seemed too improbable or uncomfortable? What was that truth, and are you willing to revisit it now with an open mind to its logical structure?
?
The atomists accepted one improbable scenario (rolling on a point) but rejected another (disjoining of the millstone). Where in your own beliefs do you hold double standards? Will you commit to examining one such inconsistency today and resolve it?
?
If you follow a logical proof to its conclusion, even if it leads to strange consequences, could it change your worldview? Are you ready to accept a truth that makes others—or even yourself—uncomfortable? Pray for the courage to embrace truth wherever it leads.

The Compass with Three Legs: Improbability vs. Impossibility
Al-Iji presents the second scenario and addresses the objection of improbability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji uses the compass scenario to illustrate that extreme improbability does not constitute a logical refutation in kalam arguments.

Core Idea
Extreme improbability does not equal impossibility in kalam reasoning.

Al-Iji introduces a second scenario: a compass with three legs, one fixed and two rotating, that draws two concentric circles. The two moving legs must rotate simultaneously; otherwise, they would separate, an event far more improbable than the millstone separation from the first scenario. The commentary quickly refutes the objection that such improbability weakens the proof, emphasizing the distinction between real impossibility and customary improbability.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation describing the compass and the simultaneous drawing of two circles.
2
Identify the argument
Notice how Al-Iji argues that the two rotating legs must move together; otherwise disjoining occurs, and this disjoining is even more improbable than in the millstone case.
3
Understand the refutation
Learn why the objection based on improbability does not invalidate the proof: real things can necessitate improbable outcomes, as shown by the example of the impossibility of a vacuum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي ثلاث فتثبت واحدة ) منها ( وتدور اثنتان حتى يرسما دائرتين الداخلية صغيرة والخارجية كبيرة، ولا شك أن هاتين الشعبتين ( يتممان ) الدائرتين معاً بحركتيهما ) وهما متلازمتان ضرورة والانفكاك بين الشعبتين ( هاهنا مع عدم التناثر والتساقط الانفكاك (أبعد) من بين أجزاء الرحى.
قوله : ( أبعد إلخ) لا شبهة في الاستبعاد لكن الأمور الحقيقية تستلزم المستبعدات كامتناع الخلاء يستلزم أموراً يستبعدها العقل استبعاداً قريباً من الاستحالة
قوله: (أبعد من الانفكاك بين أجزاء (الرحى فإن أجزاء الرحى لكونها في أحيازها لا يقتضي التناثر والتساقط لاقتضائها الأحياز التي يكون بعد الانفكاك .
And they are three: one is fixed, and two rotate so that they draw two circles, the inner small and the outer large. There is no doubt that these two legs complete the two circles together by their motions, and they are necessarily simultaneous. And the disjoining between the two legs here, without scattering and falling, the disjoining is more improbable than the disjoining between the parts of the millstone.
His saying: (more improbable etc.) There is no doubt about the improbability, but real things necessitate improbable matters, just as the impossibility of a vacuum necessitates matters that the mind finds improbable, close to impossibility.
His saying: (more improbable than the disjoining between the parts of the millstone) For the parts of the millstone, because they are in their places, do not necessitate scattering and falling, as they necessitate the places that are after disjoining.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the second scenario: a compass with three legs, one fixed and two rotating to draw two concentric circles. The argument is that the two moving legs must move simultaneously; otherwise, disjoining would occur, which is even more improbable than the previous case of the millstone. The commentary responds to an objection that such improbability does not invalidate the proof, distinguishing between real impossibility and customary improbability. The second note clarifies that the parts of the millstone, even if disjoined, do not necessarily scatter, making the admission of such disjoining less problematic. This lesson covers the second scenario and its key refutation.

Key Terms
فرجار
شعب
دائرتين
انفكاك
استبعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extreme improbability of an event (like legs separating without scattering) proves that the argument is invalid.
Correction: Al-Iji's commentary explains that real things can necessitate improbable outcomes; improbability alone does not refute a logical proof.

Checkpoint
What is the second scenario used to illustrate?
A) The impossibility of drawing two circles with a compass
B) The necessity of simultaneous motion to avoid disjoining, which is even more improbable than the millstone case
C) That a compass can only draw one circle at a time
D) That the parts of a millstone cannot disjoin

Key Takeaway
In kalam, extreme improbability does not equal impossibility; a valid proof stands even if its entailments appear unlikely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an Islamic belief that your mind labels 'improbable,' do you dismiss it, or do you pause to consider that divine realities may necessitate what seems unlikely to you? What specific belief has challenged you recently, and how does this lesson change your approach to it?
?
The impossibility of a vacuum necessitates improbable consequences. In your own life, what truth about Allah or the unseen have you struggled to accept because it felt far-fetched? Will you now trust that the proof stands, even if it leads to things your mind finds improbable?
?
This lesson forces you to confront your own intellectual arrogance: are you willing to admit that your estimation of improbability is not a valid reason to reject a well-established proof? What personal doubt or objection are you holding onto that you need to abandon in light of this distinction?

The Heel and Arm: Simultaneous Motion of Two Circles
Al-Iji presents the third scenario and its extension to celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third scenario (rotating on the heel) demonstrates that two circles drawn by different body parts must move simultaneously to avoid tearing, and how this principle applies to celestial spheres.

Core Idea
Two circles, one motion: simultaneous movement is necessary to avoid tearing.

In this lesson, we examine al-Iji's third scenario: a person rotating on his heel while stretching out his arm. This simple physical example illustrates a key argument for the simultaneous motion of different parts, which is then extended to planets moving at different distances from the pole. The passage shows how observed continuity and absence of pain prove simultaneous motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario description
Read the Arabic passage describing the third scenario: the person places his heel on the ground and rotates, drawing two circles. Then read the translation to grasp the physical setup.
Notice the two circles: small from heel, large from fingertips.
2
Understand the simultaneous motion argument
Identify the key claim: the motions of heel and fingertip are simultaneous; otherwise the person would tear apart. This is argued by observing that no pain occurs, so separation does not happen.
The argument assumes that if motion were not simultaneous, parts would separate and cause pain.
3
Apply to celestial spheres
Read the extension: assume two planets, one near the pole and one on the ecliptic, drawing circles. Their motions must also be simultaneous to avoid a breach in the spheres.
This shows the argument is not limited to physical bodies but applies to any system with coordinated motion.
4
Reflect on the necessity
Consider why al-Iji presents this scenario. It strengthens the earlier argument that parts moving together must be simultaneous to maintain continuity.
The scenario provides a sensory proof (no pain, no tearing) that simultaneous motion is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة الثالثة من وضع عقبه على الأرض ويدور على عقبه فإنه يرسم دائرتين إحداهما بعقبه ( وهي أصغر ( والأخرى بأطرافه ) أكبر، ( وإن شئت فافرضه أي الدائر على عقبه ( مادا باعه فرأس أصبعه يرسم دائرة أكبر بكثير من الدائرة التي يرسمها عقبه وحركتاهما متلازمتان لأنه إذا تحرك رأس أصبعه جزء لم يقف عقبه أصلا وإلا لزم تقطع ذلك الشخص على قياس ما مر . ( ونحن نعلم بالضرورة أنه لا ينقطع جزءاً جزءاً) كيف ؟ وتفرق الاتصال يوجب الألم مع أنه لا يجد ألماً أصلاً . ( وإن شئت فافرضه ) أي رسم الدائرة الصغيرة والكبيرة ( في الفلك في كوكبين يدور أحدهما قريب القطب والآخر على المنطقة) فإن حركتهما في رسم الدائرتين متلازمتان وإلا لزم الانخراق في الأفلاك وأن لا تكون موصوفة بالشدة والإحكام
قوله : ( كيف وتفرق إلخ) تفرق الاتصال إنما يوجب الألم إذا كان طبيعياً ولا نسلم وجوده فيما نحن فيه .
قوله : ( فإن حركتهما إلخ ) فيه أن رسم الدائرتين إذا وقعت الأجزاء على وضع واحد بحيث يتصل على هيئة الدائرة ووقوعها على هذا الوضع حال التركيب ليس ضرورياً فلا تحصل الدائرتان ولو سلم فاللازم الانفكاك وهو غير الانحراف فإنه تباعد الأجزاء بعضها عن بعض، والانفكاك لا يستلزمه ولو سلم فالانحراف جائز بل واقع عند أصحاب الجزء .
The third scenario: placing his heel on the ground and rotating on his heel, for he draws two circles, one with his heel (which is smaller) and the other with his extremities (larger). And if you wish, assume the one rotating on his heel stretches out his arm, so the tip of his finger draws a circle much larger than the circle that his heel draws, and their two motions are simultaneous, because if the tip of his finger moved a part, his heel would not stop at all, otherwise the tearing apart of that person would be necessary on the analogy of what passed. And we know by necessity that he does not tear part from part. How? And the separation of continuity causes pain, while he does not feel any pain at all. And if you wish, assume it—that is, the drawing of the small and large circle—in the celestial sphere, in two planets, one rotating near the pole and the other on the ecliptic, for their motions in drawing the two circles are simultaneous, otherwise there would be necessary a breach in the celestial spheres and that they would not be described as strong and solid.
His saying: (How and the separation etc.) The separation of continuity only causes pain if it is natural, and we do not admit its existence in what we are discussing.
His saying: (For their motions etc.) In it, that the drawing of the two circles if the parts occur in one position such that they connect in the form of a circle, and their occurrence in this position during composition is not necessary, so the two circles are not obtained; and even if admitted, the necessary is disjoining, which is not deviation, for it is the moving apart of some parts from others, and disjoining does not necessitate it; and even if admitted, deviation is permissible, rather it occurs according to the proponents of the part.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the third scenario: a person rotating on his heel draws two circles (one small from the heel, one large from the fingertips). The argument is that the two circles' motions must be simultaneous; otherwise, the person would tear apart, which we know does not happen. This is extended to celestial spheres: if planets moving at different distances from the pole did not move simultaneously, there would be a breach in the spheres. The commentary responds to two objections. First, the separation of continuity only causes pain if it is natural, which is not the case here. Second, the drawing of circles depends on a specific arrangement of parts; if that arrangement is not necessary, the argument fails, and even if disjoining occurs, it is not necessarily deviation (scattering). The proponents of the part allow deviation. This lesson covers the third scenario and its refutations.

دائرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that any separation causes pain.
Correction: The passage specifies that separation of continuity causes pain, but only when it is natural; the scenario uses the absence of pain to prove no separation occurred.

Checkpoint
What would happen if the motions of the heel and fingertip were not simultaneous?
A) The person would feel a slight discomfort
B) The person would be torn apart
C) The circles would be of equal size
D) The celestial spheres would collapse

Key Takeaway
The third scenario provides sensory proof that two circles drawn by connected body parts must have simultaneous motion, a principle that extends to celestial bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Iji uses the absence of pain as proof that your body is not torn when you rotate. What daily habits or choices demonstrate that your soul is not being torn by inconsistency? Are you aware of any 'silent pain' that might indicate an inner breach?
?
If two planets must move simultaneously to maintain the integrity of the celestial sphere, what must move simultaneously in your life to maintain your spiritual integrity? Is your ‘heel’ (foundations) aligned with your ‘fingertip’ (actions)?
?
The celestial spheres are described as 'strong and solid' (الشدة والإحكام). Is your faith equally strong and solid, or are there cracks where your beliefs and actions do not move together? What one step will you take today to restore simultaneous motion?

Objections on Pain and Disjoining in the Heel Scenario
Al-Iji responds to two objections: pain not proof of separation, and circle drawing not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the third scenario and how the responses defend the argument: pain only occurs from natural separation, and drawing circles depends on a specific arrangement that need not be necessary, with a distinction between disjoining and deviation.

Core Idea
Not all separation causes pain; disjoining is not deviation.

After presenting the third scenario, al-Iji addresses two potential objections. The first challenges the inference from absence of pain to absence of separation, noting that pain only arises from natural separation. The second objection questions whether the drawing of distinct circles requires simultaneous motion, arguing that the required configuration may not be necessary, and even if disjoining occurs, it is distinct from deviation. This lesson unpacks these objections and their refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
Read the Arabic for the first objection: 'How? And the separation of continuity causes pain...' and its response. Understand that the objector says pain only occurs when separation is natural.
The response asserts that in this scenario, the separation is not natural.
2
Read the second objection
Read the longer objection about the necessity of drawing circles. It argues that the parts must be arranged in a specific way to form circles; this arrangement is not necessary, so the argument fails. Also, even if disjoining occurs, it is not deviation.
Distinguish between 'انفكاك' (disjoining) and 'انحراف' (deviation).
3
Identify the responses
Notice that the responses are embedded in the objection. Al-Iji counters by denying that the separation is natural, and by arguing that even if disjoining happens, it does not imply deviation, and deviation is permissible according to some.
The proponents of the part allow deviation, so the objection does not refute the original claim.
4
Evaluate the overall argument
Reflect on how these objections and responses strengthen the original scenario. They show that the argument is robust against specific criticisms.
This demonstrates the dialectical method of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Euclid's Two Proofs Against the Indivisible Part
Two geometric proofs from Euclid refuting the existence of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two geometric proofs from Euclid that refute the existence of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
Geometry shows that continuous magnitudes are infinitely divisible, disproving indivisible atoms.

Euclid's Elements provides powerful arguments against the atomistic conception of space and body. The two proofs presented here show that both angles and lines can be divided infinitely, contradicting the idea of an indivisible smallest part. Let's examine them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two separate proofs labeled 'fifth' and 'sixth'.
2
Understand the first proof: angle bisection
The first proof shows a rectilinear angle can be bisected infinitely. Ask yourself: why does infinite bisection negate an indivisible part?
Think of a line or angle as continuous, not made of indivisible dots.
3
Understand the second proof: line composition
The second proof assumes a line is made of a finite number of parts. It then shows the middle part would have to be divided, contradicting its indivisibility.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the implications
These proofs are used in kalam to argue that physical bodies, like geometric magnitudes, cannot be composed of indivisible atoms.
This connects to broader theological and philosophical debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الخامس: برهن إقليدس) في المقالة الأولى من كتاب الأصول ( أن الزاوية المستقيمة الخطين قابلة للتنصيف بخط مستقيم فيكون نصفها زاوية مستقيمة الخطين قابلة للتنصيف أيضاً، وهكذا فالزاوية المستقيمة الخطين (تنقسم إلى غير النهاية، وأنه ينفي الجزء ) . الوجه ( السادس : برهن إقليدس في تلك المقالة ( على أن كل خط قابل للتنصيف، فإذا فرض ( الخط مركباً ) من أجزاء وتر كخمسة مثلاً ( لزم تجزؤ ) الجزء ( ( الوسطاني ) .
The fifth aspect: Euclid proved in the first article of the Book of the Principles that a rectilinear angle is divisible into two by a straight line, so its half is a rectilinear angle divisible into two also, and thus the rectilinear angle divides to infinity, and that negates the part. The sixth aspect: Euclid proved in that same article that every line is divisible; so if the line is assumed to be composed of parts, like five for instance, then the middle part would be split [into parts].
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs from Euclid's Elements designed to refute the existence of indivisible parts (juzʾ lā yatajazza). The first proof demonstrates that a rectilinear angle can be bisected infinitely, implying that the concept of an indivisible part is untenable. The second proof shows that if a line is composed of a finite number of parts, then the middle part would itself have to be divisible, contradicting its assumed indivisibility. Both proofs are based on the properties of geometric figures and aim to establish that infinite divisibility is a necessary property of continuous magnitudes.

Key Terms
برهان
جزء لا يتجزأ
زاوية
تنصيف
خط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs only apply to mathematical objects, not physical bodies.
Correction: In kalam, these geometric proofs are used analogically to argue that physical bodies, being continuous, cannot be composed of indivisible atoms.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof (from Euclid) about the rectilinear angle demonstrate?
A) The angle is indivisible.
B) The angle can be bisected infinitely, negating an indivisible part.
C) The angle is composed of seven parts.
D) The angle cannot be bisected.

Key Takeaway
Euclid's geometric proofs establish that continuous quantities like angles and lines are infinitely divisible, negating the concept of an indivisible part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Euclid's proofs rely on necessary truths that cannot be otherwise. When you consider the existence of Allah, do you treat it as a matter of such rational certainty, or do you relegate it to mere belief that you never examine? What would change in your life if you sought to understand the rational foundations of your faith with the same rigor as Euclid's geometry?
?
The second proof shows that assuming a finite number of parts forces one to accept the division of the middle part. In your own life, are there assumptions or habits you hold onto that, upon closer inspection, force you into contradictions? Are you willing to let go of them even if they seem foundational?
?
These geometric truths were used by Muslim scholars to defend the Islamic worldview. But knowledge of such proofs is often neglected today. Is there a branch of knowledge (like mathematics, logic, or science) that you have ignored because it seemed secular or difficult? How might mastering it strengthen your deen and your understanding of Allah's creation?

Types of Division: Actual and Mental in Kalam Reasoning
Al-Iji explains the two kinds of division: actual and mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two fundamental types of division—actual and mental—used in kalam reasoning, along with their subtypes and how they are applied.

Core Idea
Division is of two kinds: actual separation and mental separation.

In this passage, al-Iji explains a key methodological concept: how division (qisma) is classified in kalam reasoning. This classification is foundational for later arguments about the composition of bodies. We will explore the two main types and their subtypes, and note the author's advice on careful application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Pay attention to the flow from the statement about body composition to the conclusion about division.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two main types
Locate where the author states 'the division is two: actual and mental'. Note the Arabic terms: انفكاكية (infikākiyya) for actual and ذهنية (dhihniyya) for mental.
Mental division is also called imaginary (wahmiyya) and suppositional (fardiyya).
3
Understand the subtypes and usage
Notice that actual division is external, mental is conceptual. Also note the distinction between suppositional and imaginary mentioned later, and the author's emphasis on verifying usage.
The author advises diligence in applying these divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد السابع : الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة فإما بتوسط أمر باعث كما باختلاف الأعراض، أو لا يتوسطه كما بالوهم والفرض فظهر أن القسمة اثنتان انفكاكية وهي قسمة خارجية منقسمة إلى قسميها، وغير قسمة ذهنية وتسمى وهمية وفرضية أيضاً وتنقسم إلى القسمين المذكورين هذا هو الضبط وقد يفرق بين الفرضية والوهمية كما أشرنا إليه ويجعل ما باختلاف الأعراض قسيماً للوهمية المجردة كما في الكتاب فعليك بالتثبت في موارد الاستعمال.
The first observatory - the seventh goal: The body is composed of hyle and form. Either through an intermediary causing cause, as with the variation of accidents, or without it, as with imagination and supposition. So it appears that the division is two: actual (infikākiyya), which is an external division divided into its two parts, and another, mental (dhihniyya), also called imaginary (wahmiyya) and suppositional (fardiyya), and it divides into the two mentioned divisions. This is the precise formulation. And a distinction may be made between the suppositional and the imaginary as we indicated, and what is by variation of accidents is made a counterpart of pure imaginary division, as in the book. So be diligent in verifying the cases of usage.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of division (qisma) used in philosophical and kalam reasoning. It begins by stating that a division either occurs through an intermediary cause, such as the variation of accidents, or without an intermediary, as in the case of imaginary (wahmi) or suppositional (fardī) division. The main point is that there are two fundamental kinds of division: actual (infikākiyya) and mental (dhihniyya). Actual division is an external, real separation, like physically splitting a body into parts. Mental division is a conceptual separation performed in the mind, also called imaginary or suppositional division. The passage notes that these two kinds each subdivide into further types, but the author does not elaborate here. He then mentions that some scholars distinguish between suppositional (fardiyya) and imaginary (wahmiyya) division, and that they treat division based on variation of accidents as a counterpart to pure imaginary division. The author advises the reader to be diligent in verifying the application of these divisions in actual usage. This methodological discussion is foundational for the subsequent proof of hyle and form, as the argument relies on mental division (e.g., conceiving of the body as capable of separation).

Key Terms

فرضية

فرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all divisions are actual (physical) separations.
Correction: This passage clarifies that mental divisions (e.g., in imagination) are also valid and distinct from physical divisions. There are two main types: actual and mental.

Checkpoint
According to the author, into how many main types is division (qisma) classified?
A) Two: actual and mental
B) Three: actual, mental, and imaginary
C) Four: actual, mental, suppositional, and by accidents
D) One: only actual division

Key Takeaway
Division in kalam reasoning is classified into actual (infikākiyya) and mental (dhihniyya), with mental division further subdivided into imaginary and suppositional.

?

Are you careful to distinguish between what your mind divides for understanding and what is actually separate? Will you examine your assumptions today?

Correcting the Count of Celestial Spheres to Twenty-Five
Author corrects celestial sphere count to 25, refuting a commentator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author corrects the enumeration of celestial spheres from twenty-four to twenty-five within the Ptolemaic system, refuting a commentator's errors.

Core Idea
The correct count of celestial spheres is twenty-five, not twenty-four.

This lesson dives into a technical astronomical correction within the Ptolemaic framework. The author clarifies the classification of universal and partial spheres, revealing a mistake in a commentator's count. By carefully distinguishing spheres like the inclined sphere and the sphere of Jawzahar, we see how the total reaches twenty-five.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the technical terms for spheres.
2
Identify the commentator's two errors
The commentator claimed all partial spheres are epicycles or eccentrics and that the total is 24. Find where the author points out these mistakes.
Note the distinction between concentric and eccentric spheres.
3
Understand the correct enumeration
Follow the author's breakdown: 9 universal spheres + 16 partial spheres = 25. Note that the inclined sphere is counted among the universals.
4
Reflect on the refutation
See how the author's refutation hinges on proper classification. The inclined sphere being universal reconciles the count.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلم من كلامه أن الفلك الكلي القمر وهذا المائل لاشتماله على الأفلاك التي ينضبط بها حركته وأن فلك الجوزهر ليس بفلك كلي لعدم اشتماله على فلك آخر بل فلك برأسه محيط بالمائل كسائر الأفلاك لا تحتها وأثبتوا لأجل الحركة الجوزهرين وحينئذ اندفع ما أورده شارح التجريد من أن قوله وتشتمل تلك أي الأفلاك الكلية على آخر تداوير خارجة المركز، والمجموع أربعة وعشرون وفيه نظر وأما أولاً فلأنه صريح في أن الأفلاك الجزئية إنما تكون تداوير الخارجة المركز وهذا خطأ، فإن من الأفلاك الجزئية للقمر جوزهرا ومائلا، فلكان موافقان المركز وأما ثانياً : فلان عدد الأفلاك على ما هو المشهور يرتقي إلى خمسة وعشرين، لا أن الكل من المسخرة مع القمرية أو يرد أحد ، فالتداوير ستة، ولكل من السيارة فلكاً خارج المركز سوى عطارد فإن له فلكين خارجي المركز فالأفلاك الخارجة والمراكز ثمانية وللقمر فلكان آخران موافقا المركز على ما مر فعدد الأفلاك الجزئية تصير ستة عشر، وهي مع الأفلاك الكلية التسعة يرتقي إلى خمسة وعشرين ووجه الاندفاع أنه ظهر لك أن المائل المعدود في الأفلاك الكلية، فهي مع المائل تسعة وأن ليس فيما يشتمل عليه الأفلاك الكلية إلا التداوير خارجة المركز.
So it is known from his statement that the universal sphere of the Moon and this inclined [sphere] encompass the spheres by which its motion is regulated, and that the sphere of Jawzahar is not a universal sphere because it does not encompass another sphere, but is a sphere on its own surrounding the inclined sphere like other spheres, not beneath it. And they established for the sake of motion the two Jawzahars. Thus the objection raised by the commentator of al-Tajrīd is refuted, who said: 'And those [universal spheres] contain only epicycles and eccentric spheres, and the total is twenty-four.' But there is criticism: First, it is explicit that the partial spheres are only epicycles and eccentrics, which is wrong, because among the partial spheres of the Moon are Jawzahar and the inclined, two concentric spheres. Second, the number of spheres according to the well-known view reaches twenty-five, not that all are deferents with epicycles or something else. So the epicycles are six, and each of the planets has an eccentric sphere except Mercury, which has two eccentric spheres. So the eccentric spheres are eight. And the Moon has two other concentric spheres as mentioned. So the number of partial spheres becomes sixteen. Added to the nine universal spheres, it reaches twenty-five. The refutation is that it has become clear to you that the inclined sphere is counted among the universal spheres, so with the inclined they are nine, and that the universal spheres contain only epicycles and eccentrics.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a correction of the count of celestial spheres. The author first establishes that the Moon's system includes a universal sphere (falk kullī) and an inclined sphere (mā'il), and that the sphere of Jawzahar is not universal but individual. By proper enumeration, the partial spheres total sixteen (including epicycles, eccentrics, and concentric spheres for the Moon). Adding the nine universal spheres yields twenty-five. The author refutes the commentator of al-Tajrīd who claimed only twenty-four, pointing out two errors: (1) he mistakenly thought all partial spheres are epicycles or eccentrics, ignoring the Moon's two concentric spheres; (2) the correct well-known count is twenty-five. The refutation (wajh al-indifā') is that once the inclined sphere is counted among the universals, the total becomes nine, and the universals contain only epicycles and eccentrics, aligning with the correct number.

فلك جوزهر
المائل
التداوير
خارج المركز
موافق المركز
شارح التجريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute here is theological rather than astronomical.
Correction: This is a purely technical astronomical correction within the Ptolemaic framework.

Checkpoint

A) 24
B) 25
C) 16
D) 9

Key Takeaway
The correct count of celestial spheres is twenty-five, not twenty-four, when universal and partial spheres are properly identified.

?

The commentator mistakenly thought all partial spheres were epicycles or eccentrics. Is there a belief you hold that you have never thoroughly checked against the source (Qur'an and Sunnah)? Will you commit to verifying one such belief this week with the same precision as this scholar?

This correction required the author to enumerate each sphere meticulously. How carefully have you counted your own deeds—both good and bad? What would happen if you took ten minutes tonight to tally your actions today against what Allah has commanded?

Identifying the Eclipsing Body
Commentator explains how to identify the eclipsing body by color.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how astronomers determine which celestial body is eclipsing another based on color difference during an eclipse.

Core Idea
Color reveals the eclipser: the body whose color appears is the one blocking the light.

In eclipse observations, it is crucial to identify which body is the occluding one. The commentator provides a simple rule based on color: the body whose color is visible during the eclipse is the occluder. This lesson unpacks that rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the rule is stated concisely: the eclipser is known from the eclipsed by color difference.
2
Understand the rule
The body whose color appears at the moment of eclipse is the occluding body (kāsif); the other is the eclipsed (munkasif). This is a practical observational criterion.
Think about why color might differ: the occluding body is closer and blocks the light of the farther one.
3
Reflect on the significance
This rule helps astronomers assign roles during an eclipse without prior knowledge of which body is passing in front of the other.
It is a simple but effective method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجدوا إلخ) الكاسف إنما يعرف من المنكسف متى خالف لون أحدهما لون الآخر، فأيهما ظهر لونه عند الكسف يعرف أنه كاسف والآخر منكسف قوله : (اختلاف المنظر إلخ وقوس من دائرة الارتفاع بين موقعي خطين مارين بمركز الكواكب منتهيين إلى فلك البروج يخرج أحدهما من مركز العالم والثاني من موضع الإبصار . قوله : ( بذات الشعبتين) إن رصديته مركبة من ثلاثة مساطير. قوله : (متوسطة إلخ ) قال بطليموس : في المجسطي ونحن نرى ترتيب من تقادم عهده أقرب إلى الإقناع لأنه أشبه بالأمر الطبيعي لتوسط الشمس بين ما لا يبعد عنها إلا يسيراً. قوله : ( اختلاف المنظر ) وهو بعد ما بين طرفي الخطين المارين بمركز الكواكب الواصلين إلى فلك البروح، أو الفلك الأعلى، بحيث يكون أحدهما خارجاً من مركز العالم، والآخر من موضع الناظر، فإن وجد اختلاف المنظر فذلك يدل على قرب الكوكب، وإن لم يوجد، فذلك يدل على بعد الكوكب، وكذا إن كان اختلاف المنظر أكبر فهو يدل على أن الكوكب أقرب، وإن كان أصغر فهو يدل على كون الكوكب أبعد ، ثم إنهم لما وجدوا اختلاف المنظر للشمس، ولم يجدوه للعلوية ولا للثوابت حكموا بأن الشمس تحتها . قوله : ( بذات الشعبتين) هي آلة منصوبة في سطح دائرة نصف النهار، وسيجيء تفسير هذه الدائرة ويعرف بتلك الآلة أحوال اختلاف المنظر .
His saying: 'they found etc.' The eclipsing body is known from the eclipsed when the color of one differs from the color of the other; whichever appears with its color at the eclipse is known to be the eclipser and the other the eclipsed. His saying: 'parallax etc.' It is an arc of the altitude circle between the positions of two lines passing through the center of the planets, ending at the zodiac, one emanating from the center of the world and the other from the place of sight. His saying: 'with two prongs (dhāt al-shuʻbatayn)' Its observational instrument is composed of three rulers. His saying: 'intermediate etc.' Ptolemy said in the Almagest: 'We see the arrangement of those of old as more convincing because it resembles the natural order, due to the Sun being intermediate between those that do not depart far from it.' His saying: 'parallax' It is the distance between the ends of two lines passing through the centers of the planets, reaching the zodiac or the highest sphere, with one line from the center of the world and the other from the observer's position. If parallax exists, it indicates the planet is near; if not, it indicates it is far. Similarly, if parallax is greater, it indicates the planet is closer; if smaller, it indicates it is farther. Then, when they found parallax for the Sun and not for the superior planets or the fixed stars, they judged that the Sun is beneath them. His saying: 'with two prongs' It is an instrument erected in the plane of the meridian circle, and the explanation of this circle will come later, and by that instrument the conditions of parallax are known.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage gathers the commentator's glosses on technical terms from the preceding discussion. First, he clarifies how to identify the occluding vs. occluded body during an eclipse: by color difference. Then he defines parallax (ikhtilāf al-manẓar) as the arc between two lines from the planet to the zodiac, one from Earth's center and one from the observer. The instrument dhāt al-shuʻbatayn (two prongs) is described as having three rulers, used to measure parallax in the meridian plane. The commentator also reports Ptolemy's justification in the Almagest for placing the Sun between Venus/Mercury and Mars/Jupiter/Saturn, based on natural order. Finally, he reiterates the value of parallax for determining planetary distance: existence/non-existence and magnitude indicate proximity or distance. These notes provide essential technical clarification for the astronomical arguments in the main text.

المنكسف

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) By its brightness
B) By its color appearing during the eclipse
C) By its size
D) By its speed

Key Takeaway
The body whose color is visible during an eclipse is the occluding body.

Parallax: Definition and Distance Indicator
Defines parallax and explains its relationship to planetary distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of parallax in Ptolemaic astronomy and how it is used to judge a planet's distance from Earth.

Core Idea
Parallax reveals nearness: the bigger the parallax, the closer the planet is to Earth.

Parallax is a key concept in geocentric astronomy. The commentator defines it precisely as the arc between two lines from the planet to the zodiac, one from Earth's center and one from the observer's eye. This lesson explains the definition and the rule that the presence and magnitude of parallax indicate planetary distance.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of parallax
Read the two segments that define parallax. Note that it is an arc on the altitude circle between the positions of two lines from the planet to the zodiac.
The two lines are from Earth's center and from the observer's location.
2
Understand the distance rule
Parallax exists → planet is near; no parallax → planet is far. Greater parallax → closer; smaller parallax → farther.
This rule is based on the difference in viewing angle.
3
Apply to the solar system order
The commentator notes that parallax was found for the Sun but not for superior planets or fixed stars, leading to the conclusion that the Sun is beneath them.
This shows how parallax was used to determine relative distances.

The Instrument Dhāt al-Shuʻbatayn and Ptolemy's Planetary Order
Describes the instrument and Ptolemy's justification for planetary order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the design of the observational instrument called Dhāt al-Shuʻbatayn (the Instrument of Two Prongs) and how Ptolemy used it to determine the order of the planets.

Core Idea
The instrument of two prongs measures parallax and helped Ptolemy place the Sun between inferior and superior planets.

Ptolemy's Almagest describes an instrument called Dhāt al-Shuʻbatayn, composed of three rulers, used to measure parallax in the meridian plane. The commentator also reports Ptolemy's argument for the Sun's intermediate position based on the natural order. This lesson covers both the instrument and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the instrument
Note that the observational instrument (رصديته) is composed of three rulers (مساطير) and is set up in the meridian circle plane.
The name 'two prongs' likely refers to the shape of the instrument.
2
Understand Ptolemy's reasoning
Ptolemy argues that the arrangement of planets according to ancient authorities is more convincing because it resembles the natural order, with the Sun intermediate between those that do not depart far (Venus, Mercury) and the outer planets.
This appears in the Almagest.
3
Connect instrument and method
The instrument is used to measure parallax, which in turn helps determine planetary distances and order.
The commentator mentions that the conditions of parallax are known by this instrument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وجدوا إلخ) الكاسف إنما يعرف من المنكسف متى خالف لون أحدهما لون الآخر، فأيهما ظهر لونه عند الكسف يعرف أنه كاسف والآخر منكسف قوله : (اختلاف المنظر إلخ وقوس من دائرة الارتفاع بين موقعي خطين مارين بمركز الكواكب منتهيين إلى فلك البروج يخرج أحدهما من مركز العالم والثاني من موضع الإبصار . قوله : ( بذات الشعبتين) إن رصديته مركبة من ثلاثة مساطير. قوله : (متوسطة إلخ ) قال بطليموس : في المجسطي ونحن نرى ترتيب من تقادم عهده أقرب إلى الإقناع لأنه أشبه بالأمر الطبيعي لتوسط الشمس بين ما لا يبعد عنها إلا يسيراً. قوله : ( اختلاف المنظر ) وهو بعد ما بين طرفي الخطين المارين بمركز الكواكب الواصلين إلى فلك البروح، أو الفلك الأعلى، بحيث يكون أحدهما خارجاً من مركز العالم، والآخر من موضع الناظر، فإن وجد اختلاف المنظر فذلك يدل على قرب الكوكب، وإن لم يوجد، فذلك يدل على بعد الكوكب، وكذا إن كان اختلاف المنظر أكبر فهو يدل على أن الكوكب أقرب، وإن كان أصغر فهو يدل على كون الكوكب أبعد ، ثم إنهم لما وجدوا اختلاف المنظر للشمس، ولم يجدوه للعلوية ولا للثوابت حكموا بأن الشمس تحتها . قوله : ( بذات الشعبتين) هي آلة منصوبة في سطح دائرة نصف النهار، وسيجيء تفسير هذه الدائرة ويعرف بتلك الآلة أحوال اختلاف المنظر .
His saying: 'they found etc.' The eclipsing body is known from the eclipsed when the color of one differs from the color of the other; whichever appears with its color at the eclipse is known to be the eclipser and the other the eclipsed. His saying: 'parallax etc.' It is an arc of the altitude circle between the positions of two lines passing through the center of the planets, ending at the zodiac, one emanating from the center of the world and the other from the place of sight. His saying: 'with two prongs (dhāt al-shuʻbatayn)' Its observational instrument is composed of three rulers. His saying: 'intermediate etc.' Ptolemy said in the Almagest: 'We see the arrangement of those of old as more convincing because it resembles the natural order, due to the Sun being intermediate between those that do not depart far from it.' His saying: 'parallax' It is the distance between the ends of two lines passing through the centers of the planets, reaching the zodiac or the highest sphere, with one line from the center of the world and the other from the observer's position. If parallax exists, it indicates the planet is near; if not, it indicates it is far. Similarly, if parallax is greater, it indicates the planet is closer; if smaller, it indicates it is farther. Then, when they found parallax for the Sun and not for the superior planets or the fixed stars, they judged that the Sun is beneath them. His saying: 'with two prongs' It is an instrument erected in the plane of the meridian circle, and the explanation of this circle will come later, and by that instrument the conditions of parallax are known.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage gathers the commentator's glosses on technical terms from the preceding discussion. First, he clarifies how to identify the occluding vs. occluded body during an eclipse: by color difference. Then he defines parallax (ikhtilāf al-manẓar) as the arc between two lines from the planet to the zodiac, one from Earth's center and one from the observer. The instrument dhāt al-shuʻbatayn (two prongs) is described as having three rulers, used to measure parallax in the meridian plane. The commentator also reports Ptolemy's justification in the Almagest for placing the Sun between Venus/Mercury and Mars/Jupiter/Saturn, based on natural order. Finally, he reiterates the value of parallax for determining planetary distance: existence/non-existence and magnitude indicate proximity or distance. These notes provide essential technical clarification for the astronomical arguments in the main text.

دائرة نصف النهار

B) Three rulers

D) A single rod

Key Takeaway
The instrument of two prongs is a three-ruler device in the meridian plane used to measure parallax, supporting Ptolemy's natural planetary order.

Determining the Order of Planets through Occultation
Explains how lower occult higher to determine planetary order, and the limitation for Mercury and Venus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how astronomers used occultation to determine the order of celestial bodies and why the relative position of the Sun, Venus, and Mercury remained uncertain.

Core Idea
Occultation reveals planetary order, except when planets are lost in the Sun's glare.

Astronomers observed that lower bodies occult higher ones, allowing them to deduce the planetary order. The Moon occults all planets, indicating it is the lowest. Mercury occults Venus, Venus occults Mars, and so on. However, the Sun's brightness and its proximity to Mercury and Venus prevented a definitive determination using occultation alone, forcing Ptolemy to resort to approximation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's flow come through before analyzing.
2
Identify the principle of occultation
Find where the author states the principle that a lower body hides a higher one when aligned.
The key term is 'حجب' (occultation).
3
List the empirical findings
Go through the list of occultations: Moon occults all, Mercury occults Venus, etc. Notice the order.
This establishes the sequence.
4
Understand the Sun's special case
Note that the Sun is only eclipsed by the Moon, and it has parallax, placing it between Moon and superior planets.
Parallax (اختلاف المنظر) is a key indicator.
5
Recognize the limitation
The passage explains why the Sun's relation to Venus and Mercury cannot be determined via occultation—they are hidden by sunlight.
Ptolemy's resort to 'طريقة الاستحسان' (approximation) shows the limit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما هو أسفل يحجب ما هو أعلى أي يصير ساتراً له عنا إذا وقع على محاذاته ( وهو) أي الحجب ) على ما ذكرنا من الترتيب ) فإنهم وجدوا القمر يحجب سائر السيارة. ومن الثوابت ما هو على طريقته. فعلم أنه تحت الجميع ووجدوا عطارداً يكسف الزهرة، والزهرة المريخ والمريخ المشتري والمشتري زحل، وزحل بعض الثوابت، وأما الشمس فإنها لا تنكسف إلا بالقمر ولا يتصور كسفها بشيء من الكواكب لأنها تستتر بشعاعها إذا قربت منها، لكن لها اختلاف المنظر دون العلوية فهي تحتها وفوق القمر، وبقي الاشتباه في أنها فوق الزهرة وعطارد أو تحتها إذ لا سبيل إلى معرفة ذلك من الكسف لما عرفت من احتراقهما تحت الشعاع عند القرآن اختلاف المنظر لأنهما لا يبعدان عن الشمس كثيرا بعد، فلا يظهران عند كونهما على نصف النهار ليعلم بذات الشعبتين المنصوبة في سطح نصف النهار، أن لها اختلاف منظر أو لا، فلذلك عدل بطليموس إلى طريقة الاستحسان .
For what is lower obscures what is higher, i.e., it becomes a veil for it from us when it falls in its alignment. And (the obscuration) according to the order we mentioned. They found that the moon obscures all the planets. And among the fixed stars there is what is on its path. So they knew that it is beneath all. And they found that Mercury eclipses Venus, Venus eclipses Mars, Mars eclipses Jupiter, Jupiter eclipses Saturn, and Saturn eclipses some of the fixed stars. As for the sun, it is not eclipsed except by the moon, and it is inconceivable that it be eclipsed by any of the planets because it is veiled by its own rays when they approach it. However, it has parallax (ikhtilaf al-manzar) unlike the superior [planets], so it is beneath them and above the moon. And the uncertainty remained whether it is above Venus and Mercury or below them, since there is no way to know that from eclipses because of what you know of their being burnt under the rays during conjunction? parallax? because they do not go far from the sun much, so they do not appear when they are at noon to be known by the instrument 'dhat al-shu'batayn' erected in the plane of the meridian, whether they have parallax or not. Therefore Ptolemy turned to the method of approximation (istiḥsān).
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of occultation (ḥajb) as a method to determine the order of celestial bodies. It states that a lower body obscures a higher one when they align. Then it lists empirical findings: the Moon obscures all planets; Mercury eclipses Venus, etc. It notes that the Sun is only eclipsed by the Moon, not by other planets because its brightness hides them. The Sun has parallax, indicating it is below the superior planets but above the Moon. However, it is uncertain whether the Sun is above or below Venus and Mercury because those planets are too close to the Sun to be observed at noon for parallax measurement. Hence Ptolemy resorted to a method of approximation (istiḥsān). The passage follows the author's argument: first presenting the method, then applying it, then noting the limitation that forces an alternative method for Mercury and Venus.

Key Terms
حجب
كسف
اختلاف المنظر
طريقة الاستحسان
ذات الشعبتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Occultation can determine the order of all planets without exception.
Correction: Occultation fails for planets too close to the Sun due to its brightness, requiring alternative methods.

Checkpoint
Why could the order of Venus and Mercury relative to the Sun not be determined by occultation?
A) Because they are too far from the Sun
B) Because they are never in opposition
C) Because they are too close to the Sun and disappear in its rays
D) Because they have no parallax

Key Takeaway
Occultation reveals the order of planets down to the Moon, but the Sun's brilliance hides Mercury and Venus, so their positions relative to the Sun remain uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing, like Ptolemy, to seek an alternative path when direct observation fails, or do you stubbornly cling to your first approach?
?
The Sun's brilliance hides Mercury and Venus from view. What 'sun' in your life—your pride, comfort, or habits—might be obscuring a truth you need to see about your relationship with Allah? What step will you take this week to shift your perspective so that the hidden becomes visible?
?
Ptolemy did not hesitate to admit his uncertainty about the Sun's position relative to Mercury and Venus. How comfortable are you with saying 'I do not know' before your Creator? What would it cost you to admit ignorance in a matter of faith, and what would it free you to pursue?

Ptolemy's Necklace Analogy and Ibn Sina's Observation
Ptolemy's necklace analogy and Ibn Sina's observation of Venus on the sun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ptolemy's view of planetary order was supported by empirical observations and later challenged.

Core Idea
The sun is the central bead in the planetary necklace, but is Venus really a mole on its face?

This passage presents Ptolemy's elegant analogy comparing the sun to a bead in a necklace, centrally placed among the planets. It then reports Ibn Sina's empirical claim that Venus appears as a spot on the sun during conjunction, which was later disputed by engineers who suggested the spot is a permanent feature. This lesson explores the interplay between theoretical models and observational evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to capture the author's flow.
Notice the use of parentheses in the Arabic, indicating interruptions in the argument.
2
Identify Ptolemy's necklace analogy
Find the phrase 'كسمة القلادة' and understand the planetary order: the sun is intermediate between superior and inferior planets and the moon.
3
Examine the empirical evidence
Ibn Sina and others claimed to see Venus as a mole on the sun. Note that some claimed to see both Venus and Mercury as two moles.
This empirical claim supports Ptolemy's model.
4
Understand the refutation
Engineers argued that Venus's sphere is below Mercury's and above the sun, and they denied Ibn Sina's observation. They explained that a permanent black spot on the sun could be mistaken for Venus.
This shows how a single observation can be reinterpreted.
5
Reflect on methodology
Consider how this passage illustrates the tension between theoretical models and empirical claims. What does it teach about scientific reasoning?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : هي كسمة القلادة متوسطة بين السبعة السيارة أعني بين العلوية وبين السفليتين والقمر. وقد تأكد هذا الرأي بما ذكره بعض المتأخرين كابن سينا ومن تقدمه من مقدمي هذه الصناعة أنه رأى الزهرة عند اجتماعها مع الشمس كشامة على صفحتها ومنهم من ادعى أنه رآها وعطارداً كشامتين عليها ( وقد زعم بعض المهندسين أن فلك الزهرة ) دون فلك عطارد ( فوق فلك الشمس وكذب ) ذلك البعض ( ابن سينا فيما زعم أنه رأى الزهرة في وجه الشمس كالشامة ) فإنه قد زعم بعض الناس أن في وجه الشمس نقطة سوداء فوق مركزها بقليل كالمحو في وجه القمر فهذه النقطة هي الشامة، وأما الشامتين فجاز أن تكون إحداهما هذه النقطة والأخرى عطارداً.
He said: It is like a necklace bead, intermediate among the seven planets, i.e., between the superior ones and the two inferior ones and the moon. And this opinion was confirmed by what some later scholars mentioned, such as Ibn Sina and those before him among the pioneers of this craft, that he saw Venus at its conjunction with the sun as a mole on its disc. And among them there is one who claimed that he saw it and Mercury as two moles on it. (And some engineers claimed that the sphere of Venus) beneath the sphere of Mercury (above the sphere of the sun, and that person denied) Ibn Sina regarding what he claimed that he saw Venus on the face of the sun as a mole. For some people claimed that on the face of the sun there is a black spot slightly above its center like a blemish on the face of the moon, and that spot is the mole. As for the two moles, it is possible that one of them is this spot and the other is Mercury.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents Ptolemy's hypothesis that the Sun is placed centrally among the planets, like a bead in a necklace, between the superior planets (Mars, Jupiter, Saturn) and the inferior ones (Venus, Mercury) and the Moon. Then it notes that this view was strengthened by later observations: Ibn Sina and others claimed to have seen Venus as a spot on the Sun during conjunction, and some even claimed to see both Venus and Mercury as two spots. However, the passage also records a refutation: some engineers argued that Venus's sphere is below Mercury's and above the Sun, and they rejected Ibn Sina's claim. They suggested that a permanent black spot on the Sun could be mistaken for Venus. The passage thus presents both the supportive empirical evidence and its criticism, following the author's method of presenting and then disputing claims.

شامة
مهندسين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's claim that the spot on the sun is Venus.
Correction: The spot is a permanent black spot on the sun, not Venus.

Checkpoint

A) It was actually a permanent black spot on the sun
B) It was a reflection
C) It was Mercury
D) It was an optical illusion

Key Takeaway
Ptolemy's view was supported by empirical claims, but those claims were later disputed by suggesting the observed spot was a permanent feature.

?
