The Nine Celestial Spheres and Their Proof
Enumerates the nine spheres and provides proofs for multiple spheres against alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional argument for the existence of nine celestial spheres and how alternative models are systematically refuted.

Core Idea
The spheres' impenetrability forces multiple spheres, as simpler alternatives fail.

This passage from the Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the classic Ash'ari proof for the multiplicity of the celestial spheres. It first lists the nine spheres, then establishes the key premise that spheres cannot interpenetrate. From there, it examines several alternative models—such as planets moving like swimmers, or rings within a single sphere—and shows that each is either impossible or no simpler than the accepted system. This step-by-step refutation solidifies the necessity of the nine-sphere cosmos.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of spheres
Start by reading the first part of the passage, which lists the nine universal spheres (seven planetary spheres, the fixed stars, and the ninth sphere). Notice that each planet itself has multiple spheres.
The ninth sphere is the outermost, responsible for the daily motion.
2
Understand the foundational premise
The proof rests on the idea that spheres cannot penetrate each other. If they could, we could have a single stationary sphere with moving planets. Since they cannot, we need multiple spheres.
This premise is crucial for the entire argument.
3
Examine the first objection (rings)
The text asks: why not have planets on rings moving within a single sphere? The author argues this is no less far-fetched than accepting eccentrics and epicycles, which are already used in astronomy.
The ring model would still require complex mechanisms.
4
Examine the second objection (single soul)
Another objection: why not have a single soul move all eight spheres with daily motion, eliminating the need for a ninth sphere? The author notes this would still require separate souls for each sphere's proper motion.
This shows the ninth sphere is not easily discarded.
5
Consider the third objection (reduce to seven)
Some argued even the eighth sphere is unnecessary by placing fixed stars on Saturn's sphere. The author presents this as the most extreme simplification, leaving only seven spheres.
This extreme view is mentioned but not endorsed; it shows the limits of simplification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه التسعة التي ذكرناها هي الأفلاك الكلية ثم إن كل واحد من فلك الأفلاك، وفلك الثوابت كرة واحدة ولكل من السيارة عدة أفلاك يتركب منها فلكه الكلي، وسنعدها عليك عداً إن شاء الله تعالى ومبناه ) أي مبنى ما ذكر من الدليل على تعدد الأفلاك هو ( أن الأفلاك لا تنخرق ( أصلاً ) وإلا جاز أن يكون هناك فلك واحد ساكن، ويكون ( الحركة للكوكب نفسه كالسابح في الماء، وإن سلم ذلك) أي امتناع الانخراق ( فلم لا يجوز أن تكون الكواكب على نطاقات ) أي أجسام شبيهة بحلق يكون تحتها مساوياً لأقطار الكواكب المركوزة فيها ( تتحرك ) تلك النطاقات ( إما بنفسها أو باعتماد الكواكب عليها ) وتكون تلك النطاقات بأسرها مغرقة في كرة واحدة على أوضاع مختلفة ) وليس ذلك ) أي إثبات النطاقات والحركة عليها ( بأبعد من إثبات ( الخارج ( المركز ( ومتمميه ( المختلفي الثخن والوضع . ( ثم ) إن سلمنا أن ذلك غير جائز ، قلنا : (لم) لا يجوز أن يكون للكل من حيث هو كل ( حركة غير حركة كل واحد وتكون هي أي حركة الكل (الحركة اليومية ) الشاملة لجميع الكواكب (فيغني ) هذا الذي ذكرناه ( عن إثبات ( الفلك ( التاسع) وذلك بأن تتعلق نفس واحدة بمجموع الأفلاك الثمانية وتحركه هذه الحركة السريعة وتتعلق بكل واحد منها نفس على حدة وتحركه حركة أخرى فينتظم حال الحركات المرصودة بلا حاجة إلى فلك تاسع. وقد زاد بعضهم على ذلك وقال : لا حاجة حينئذ إلى الثامن أيضاً لجواز فرض الثوابت ودوائر البروج على ممثل زحل، فتكون الأفلاك الكلية سبعة
So these nine which we have mentioned are the universal spheres. Then each one of the sphere of the spheres and the sphere of the fixed stars is a single sphere, and for each of the planets there are several spheres from which its universal sphere is composed, and we will enumerate them for you one by one if God wills. And its foundation, i.e., the foundation of the proof mentioned for the multiplicity of spheres, is that the spheres do not penetrate each other at all. Otherwise, it would be possible that there be a single stationary sphere, and the motion be for the planet itself like a swimmer in water. And if that is granted, i.e., the impossibility of penetration, then why is it not permissible that the planets be on rings (niṭāqāt), i.e., bodies similar to rings beneath which is a space equal to the diameters of the planets fixed in them, those rings moving either by themselves or by the planets depending on them, and those rings as a whole immersed in a single sphere in different positions? And that, i.e., positing rings and motion upon them, is not more far-fetched than positing the eccentric (al-khārij al-markaz) and its complements (mutamimmīhi) of varying thickness and position. Then, if we grant that that is not permissible, we say: why is it not permissible that for the whole, qua whole, there be a motion other than the motion of each one, and that motion of the whole be the daily motion comprehensive of all planets, and this would suffice for positing a ninth sphere? That is by having one soul (nafs) attach to the entirety of the eight spheres and move them this rapid motion, and attach to each one of them a separate soul and move it with another motion, so the state of the observed motions is arranged without need for a ninth sphere. And some added to that and said: then there is no need for the eighth sphere either, because it is permissible to posit the fixed stars and the zodiacal circles on the sphere of Saturn, so the universal spheres would be seven.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage first enumerates the nine universal spheres (al-aflāk al-kulliyya): the eight planetary spheres (including the sphere of the fixed stars as the eighth) and the ninth sphere (the outermost daily motion sphere). Then it specifies that the sphere of the fixed stars is a single sphere, but each planet has multiple spheres that compose its total sphere. The author then begins a proof for the multiplicity of spheres. The foundation of the proof is that spheres cannot penetrate each other (lā tankhariq). If they could, one could have a single stationary sphere with planets moving like swimmers. But since penetration is impossible, the author considers alternatives: why not have planets mounted on rings moving within a single sphere? He argues that this is no more far-fetched than the accepted concepts of eccentric and epicycle. Then he raises another objection: even if rings are rejected, why not have a single daily motion for the whole system, with separate souls for each sphere? This would eliminate the need for a ninth sphere. Some even went further and said the eighth sphere (of fixed stars) is also unnecessary by placing the fixed stars on Saturn's sphere, reducing the universal spheres to seven. The passage presents these objections to force a rigorous defense of the traditional nine-sphere model. The author's method is to list possible alternatives and argue against them, often by showing they are as plausible as the accepted eccentricities.

انخراق
نطاقات
خارج المركز

حركة يومية
نفس
فلك تاسع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ninth sphere is unnecessary because a single soul could move all spheres with daily motion.
Correction: The passage shows that eliminating the ninth sphere still requires separate souls for each sphere's proper motion, so the system is not simplified.

Checkpoint
What is the foundational premise for the proof of multiple spheres mentioned in the passage?
A) The spheres are solid and cannot interpenetrate
B) The planets move by themselves
C) The daily motion is the fastest
D) The fixed stars are on the eighth sphere

Key Takeaway
The necessity of nine celestial spheres is proven by the impossibility of spheres interpenetrating and the failure of simpler alternative models.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the night sky, do you see only a flat canvas, or do you ponder the intricate layers of spheres that this lesson describes? What does your perception of the cosmos reveal about your willingness to embrace complexity? This lesson challenges you to move beyond superficial understanding. Will you allow yourself to be unsettled by the depth of creation?
?
The author tests the necessity of multiple spheres by imagining alternatives. In your own beliefs, do you avoid testing them for fear they may not hold? What is one belief you hold that you have never subjected to honest scrutiny? This lesson calls you to follow the method of inquiry, not to rest in comfortable assumptions. Will you take one belief and test it today?
?
The passage leaves some objections unanswered; it does not force a conclusion but invites reflection. Does your relationship with truth allow for unanswered questions, or do you demand closure that silences inquiry? How can you cultivate a faith that is strong enough to entertain doubt and deep enough to emerge more certain?

Division of the Zodiacal Belt into Twelve Signs and Their Subdivisions
The author enumerates the divisions of the zodiacal belt into signs and their sexagesimal subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical astronomers divided the zodiacal belt into 12 signs, each further subdivided into 30 degrees, 60 minutes, and beyond using a sexagesimal system.

Core Idea
The zodiac is divided into 12 signs, each subdivided into 30 degrees, 60 minutes, and so on, to simplify calculation.

In this passage, the author explains the systematic division of the zodiacal belt (mintaqat al-burūj) into twelve equal signs, each further subdivided into degrees, minutes, and seconds. This sexagesimal system was chosen because most fractions yield integers, easing astronomical calculations. Understanding this division is essential for kalam arguments that involve celestial motions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the division proceeds step by step from the whole belt to the smallest fractions.
2
Identify each level of division
List the levels: four quarters of the belt → three each to make 12 signs → each sign into 30 degrees → each degree into 60 minutes → each minute into 60 seconds → thirds → fourths, etc.
The system is sexagesimal (base-60).
3
Understand the reason for 30 degrees
The commentator (not in the passage) explains that 30 was chosen because most fractions come out as integers, simplifying calculation. Consider why this practical consideration matters.
4
Connect to kalam
Reflect on how this technical division is used in broader theological arguments that reference the heavens.
This precision reflects the orderliness of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قسموا كل قسم من الأقسام الأربعة ثلاثة أقسام متساوية فيكون المجموع أي مجموع منطقة البروج منقسماً إلى اثني عشر قسماً وتوهموا ست دوائر عظام تتقاطع على قطبي البروج وتمر كل واحدة منها برأسي قسمين متقابلين من تلك الأقسام وحينئذ يفصل بين كل قسمين منها نصف دائرة من تلك الدوائر فيحيط بها أي بالأقسام كلها ست دوائر كما عرفت وسموا كل قسم من الاثني عشر برجاً ثم قسموا كل برج ثلاثين قسماً سواء وسموها درجاً وقسموا كل درجة ستين قسماً سواء وسموها دقائق وقسموا الدقائق أي كل واحدة منها ستين قسماً متساوية وسموها ثواني وهكذا قسموا الثواني وسموها ثوالث وقسموا الثوالث وسموها روابع فما زاد مما يمكن اعتباره من الكسور، وكما أن كل قطعة من منطقة البروج واقعة بين نصفي دائرتين تسمى برجاً كذلك القطع الواقعة من سطح الفلك الأعلى بين أنصاف تلك الدوائر على هيئة جراب البطيخ تسمى بروجاً. فعلى هذا يكون طول كل برج فيما بين المغرب والمشرق ثلاثين درجة وعرضه مائة وثمانين درجة. قوله : ( كل قسم إلخ) أي كل ربع من أرباع منطقة البروج المتوهمة على سطح الفلك الأعلى. قوله : ( ثلاثين قسماً) بناءً على أن أكثر الكسور يخرج منه صحيحاً فيسهل الحساب قوله : (درجاً) كالشمس يظهر فيها ويهبط وأجزاء سائر الدوائر تسمى أجزاء . قوله : ( وكما أن كل قطعة من منطقة إلخ) كما يدل عليه كلام المصنف . فإنه قال : سموا كل قسم برجاً . قوله : ( كذلك القطع (الواقعة) هذا هو الإطلاق المشهور وقد صرح بإطلاقين في التذكرة . قوله : ( وعرضه مائة وثمانون (درجة يعني أن عرض ما بين القطبين، فعلى هذا يكون طول کل برج سدس عرضه ستة أمثال طوله .
Then they divided each of the four sections into three equal sections, so the total—that is, the total of the zodiacal belt (mintaqat al-burūj)—is divided into twelve sections. And they imagined six great circles intersecting at the poles of the zodiac, each one passing through the heads of two opposite sections. Then between every two sections a half-circle from those circles separates them, so it encompasses—that is, all the sections—six circles, as you know. And they named each of the twelve sections a sign (burj). Then they divided each sign into thirty equal parts and called them degrees (daraj). And they divided each degree into sixty equal parts and called them minutes (daqāʼiq). And they divided the minutes—that is, each one of them—into sixty equal parts and called them seconds (thawānī). And likewise they divided the seconds and called them thirds (thawālith). And they divided the thirds and called them fourths (rawābiʻ), and so on in whatever fractions can be considered. And just as every piece of the zodiacal belt lying between two half-circles is called a sign, likewise the pieces lying on the surface of the highest sphere between the halves of those circles, in the form of a melon-slice, are called signs. Accordingly, the length of each sign between west and east is thirty degrees, and its width is one hundred and eighty degrees. His saying: 'Every section etc.' i.e., every quarter of the four quarters of the imaginary zodiacal belt on the surface of the highest sphere. His saying: 'thirty parts' is based on the fact that most fractions come out of it as integers, simplifying calculation. His saying: 'degrees' like the sun appears and descends in them, while parts of other circles are called parts. His saying: 'And just as every piece of the belt etc.' as indicated by the author's words, for he said: 'They named each section a sign.' His saying: 'likewise the pieces (falling)' this is the common usage, and two usages are stated in al-Tadhkira. His saying: 'and its width one hundred and eighty (degrees)' means the width between the two poles, so accordingly the length of each sign is one-sixth of its width, its width being six times its length.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines the technical divisions of the zodiacal belt (mintaqat al-burūj) used in astronomy and kalam cosmology. The author first describes how each quarter of the belt is subdivided into three equal parts, making twelve total. Then six great circles are imagined intersecting at the zodiacal poles, effectively creating boundaries for the signs. Each of the twelve sections is called a sign (burj). He then defines the subdivisions: each sign is 30 degrees; each degree is 60 minutes; each minute is 60 seconds; and so on into thirds, fourths, etc. This hierarchical sexagesimal system is chosen because most fractions yield integers, easing calculation. The author then clarifies that the term 'burj' applies both to arcs on the belt and to the corresponding three-dimensional slices of the highest sphere, like melon slices. He gives the dimensions: each sign spans 30 degrees in longitude and 180 degrees in latitude (from pole to pole). The commentary explains each phrase: 'each section' means each quarter of the belt; the 30-part division for calculation ease; the term 'degree' is specific to solar motion, while other circles use 'parts'; and the two usages of 'burj' are noted from al-Tadhkira. The width is the distance between the poles, making length one-sixth of width.

According to the passage, how many signs are in the zodiacal belt?
A) 4

Key Takeaway
The zodiacal belt is divided into 12 signs, each with 30 degrees, 60 minutes, etc., forming a precise sexagesimal system for astronomical measurement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes a meticulous system of division—each sign into degrees, minutes, seconds, and beyond. As you read, did you feel a sense of awe at the precision? Now consider: how meticulously do you divide your own daily schedule for acts of worship? What single adjustment could you make today to introduce more precision in your prayer times or Quran recitation?
?
The text says the scholars 'imagined' six great circles to separate the signs. They used their intellect to create a conceptual framework. In your own understanding of Allah's creation, are there areas where you have accepted 'imagined' ideas without verifying them against revelation? Identify one such concept and commit to researching what the Quran and Sunnah actually say about it this week.
?
The subdivisions continue 'and so on'—there is no end to the fractions. This mirrors the infinite precision of Allah's creation. How does this impact your belief in Allah's infinite knowledge? When you face a situation you cannot measure or fully understand, will you trust that Allah's knowledge encompasses every 'fourth' and beyond? Write a short prayer asking Allah to increase your trust in His comprehensive knowledge.

The Shape and Dimensions of the Zodiacal Signs
The author describes the three-dimensional shape of zodiacal signs and gives their dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the zodiacal signs are not just arcs on a belt but three-dimensional slices on the highest sphere, and you will know their length and width.

Core Idea
Each sign is a melon-like slice on the highest sphere, spanning 30 degrees in length and 180 degrees in width.

After establishing the numerical divisions, the passage now explains the three-dimensional reality of the signs. They are not merely arcs on a circle but solid sections of the celestial sphere, shaped like melon slices. The author also gives the dimensions: each sign is 30 degrees long (east-west) and 180 degrees wide (north-south), from pole to pole. This clarifies the spatial representation of the zodiac in classical astronomy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the analogy to melon slices (جراب البطيخ).
2
Understand the shape
The signs are like slices of the highest sphere, bounded by half-circles from pole to pole.
3
Find the dimensions
Each sign has length (30 degrees) and width (180 degrees). Length is east-west, width is north-south.
4
Connect to the overall system
Reflect how this three-dimensional understanding complements the earlier numerical division.
The commentator notes the width is the distance between poles.

Why Zodiac Signs Keep Their Names Despite Stellar Drift
The author explains the persistence of zodiac sign names despite precession and infers the recency of Greek stellar kinematics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the twelve zodiac signs (burūj) retain their names despite the gradual precession of the fixed stars, and how this reveals the historical development of astronomical knowledge.

Core Idea
The zodiac signs are fixed divisions; constellations shift, but names remain to avoid confusion.

We often confuse the zodiac signs with the star patterns they are named after. In this passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif, the author explains that the signs are fixed divisions of the ninth sphere, while the constellations on the eighth sphere slowly drift due to precession. The names, however, were kept unchanged to prevent confusion. The passage also uses this precession to argue that the Greeks' knowledge of stellar motion was recent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow with the literal translation. Pay attention to the distinction between the ninth sphere (fixed signs) and the eighth sphere (moving constellations).
The passage is technical; take your time.
2
Identify the reason for keeping the names
Notice that the text says the names were not changed 'to avoid confusion' (كيلا يؤدي إلى الالتباس). This is the key practical reason.
3
Examine the observational example
The author gives an example: the star of Aries (kawkab al-ḥamal) is now at the 23rd degree of Cancer, and Gemini's feet have shifted. This illustrates precession in action.
The phrase 'which is Cancer' is ambiguous; it likely means the star originally in Aries now appears in Cancer.
4
Understand the inference about Greek knowledge
The text argues that because the shift is only about one cycle, the Greeks' knowledge of stellar motion must be recent. If it were ancient, the shift would have accumulated over multiple cycles.
5
Reflect on the tradition about Adam
The passage ends with a tradition about star positions at Adam's time. This connects astronomical observations to Islamic cosmology.
This tradition is from al-Nihāya and serves as a supplementary reference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذوا أسماء البروج الاثني عشر المشهورة من صور تخيلوها من وصل الخطوط بين كواكب من الثوابت كانت موازية لها حين التسمية وإنها أي تلك الصور المتخيلة تزول عن موازاة البروج بالحركة البطيئة التي للثوابت والأسماء بحالها فإن البروج أقسام للفلك التاسع ولا شك أن تلك الصور على الفلك الثامن فلا بد من خروجها عن الموازاة بحركته البطيئة فكان ينبغي تغيير الأسماء إلا أنهم لم يغيروها كيلا يؤدي إلى الالتباس. قوله : ( من خروجها عن (الموازاة كما في زماننا هذا فإن كوكب الحمل وهو السرطان بلغ إلى الدرجة الثالثة والعشرين منه وسيبلغ إلى بروج الثور، وقد نفى في صورة التوءمين في بروجها أقدامهما وفيه دلالة أن العلم الذي استفاد منه اليونانيون من معرفة حركة الثوابت محدث إذ لو كان قديماً لاستحال أن ينقل كواكب صورة البروج ومع دور فضلاً عن دور أو أكثر ولم يعلموا أنها متحركة على ما تقدم من أن القدماء يعتقدونها ثابتة وقد قيل : إن وقت هبوط آدم عليه السلام من الجنة كان قلب الأسد في الجوزاء أو نسر الطائر في العقرب والعيوق في أوائل الحمل كذا في النهاية .
And they took the names of the twelve famous signs (al-burūj) from images they imagined by connecting lines between fixed stars (thawābit) that were aligned with them at the time of naming. And those imagined images depart from alignment with the signs due to the slow motion of the fixed stars, while the names remain as they are, for the signs are divisions of the ninth sphere, and there is no doubt that those images are on the eighth sphere, so they must depart from alignment due to its slow motion. So it would have been necessary to change the names, but they did not change them to avoid confusion. His saying: 'from their departure from alignment' as in our time, for the star of Aries (kawkab al-ḥamal) which is Cancer (al-saraṭān) has reached its twenty-third degree and will reach the signs of Taurus; and the feet of the image of Gemini have been removed from its signs. And this indicates that the knowledge that the Greeks derived from knowing the motion of the fixed stars is recent, for if it were ancient, it would be impossible for the stars of the sign images to shift one cycle, let alone more, and they did not know they move, as previously stated that the ancients believed them fixed. And it is said: at the time of Adam's descent from Paradise, the Heart of the Lion was in Gemini, or the Flying Eagle in Scorpio, and Capella in early Aries—so in al-Nihāya.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage discusses the relationship between the zodiac signs (al-burūj) as fixed divisions of the ninth sphere and the constellations formed by connecting fixed stars on the eighth sphere. The authors of the system borrowed the names of the twelve signs from imaginary pictures of stars that were aligned at the time, but due to precession (the slow motion of the fixed stars), the constellations have drifted away from their original positions in the zodiac. Nevertheless, the names remain attached to the fixed divisions to avoid confusion. The commentator gives an observational example: in his time, the star of Aries (kawkab al-ḥamal) is actually located in the twenty-third degree of what? The text says 'which is Cancer'—this is a difficult point; perhaps it means the star that was originally in Aries now appears in Cancer due to precession. Likewise, the feet of Gemini have shifted out of the Gemini sign. The commentator infers from this that Greek astronomical knowledge of proper motion is recent (limited), because if it were ancient, the shift would have been more than one cycle. He also cites a tradition about the positions of stars at the time of Adam.

Key Terms
بروج (burūj)
ثوابت (thawābit)
حركة بطيئة (slow motion)
فلك تاسع (ninth sphere)
فلك ثامن (eighth sphere)
التباس (confusion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Zodiac signs are the actual star groups.
Correction: The zodiac signs are fixed divisions of the ninth sphere whose names were borrowed from imagined constellations; the constellations themselves drift due to precession.

Checkpoint
Why did the astronomers not change the names of the signs when the constellations drifted?
A) They did not notice the drift.
B) To avoid confusion in tracking motions.
C) They believed the constellations were fixed.
D) The names were sacred.

Key Takeaway
The zodiac signs are fixed celestial divisions whose names outlast the constellations that inspired them, illustrating both astronomical consistency and the late recognition of stellar motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author notes that the names of the zodiac signs were kept to avoid confusion even though the constellations shifted. Reflect on a name or label you hold onto in your own life—whether it defines your identity, your reputation, or your understanding of someone else—that may no longer match the reality. Are you clinging to it out of convenience or fear of confusion? What step can you take today to align your perception with the truth?
?
The Greeks did not realize the fixed stars moved because they assumed ancient knowledge was complete. Where in your spiritual life have you assumed that what you already know is enough, closing yourself off to new insights? What is one area where you need to humbly acknowledge your ignorance and seek deeper understanding?
?
The passage references the positions of stars at the time of Adam's descent, linking celestial events to prophetic history. This suggests that even seemingly distant astronomical facts are connected to our faith. How does this perspective change the way you view ordinary events or natural phenomena? Will you begin to look for signs of divine wisdom in the world around you, starting today?

Why the Zodiac Starts at the Vernal Equinox
The author explains why the zodiac begins at the vernal equinox and groups signs into four seasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the zodiac cycle begins at the vernal equinox and how the twelve signs are grouped into four seasons based on the sun's position.

Core Idea
The zodiac begins at the vernal equinox because it marks the rebirth of growth.

Have you ever wondered why the zodiac signs are associated with seasons? This passage explains the practical logic behind starting the zodiac cycle at the vernal equinox, the moment when spring begins and life reawakens. The signs are then grouped into four seasonal trios based on the sun's journey through the equinoxes and solstices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the sequential structure: start, then three signs per season.
2
Identify the starting point reasoning
Find why the vernal equinox is chosen. Look for the phrase about growth and fruits.
3
Map the seasonal groups
List the three signs for each season as given: spring, summer, autumn, winter.
Memorize the trios: Aries-Taurus-Gemini for spring, etc.
4
Understand the commentator's note
Read the sentence 'فصارت إلخ' and see how it connects to the idea of a third circle after the two belts.
5
Reflect on the seasonal definition
Notice that the seasons are defined by the sun's position, not by weather. This is a key astronomical concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وابتدؤوا في اعتبار البروج وافتتاح الدور بما يلي الاعتدال الربيعي من جانب الشمال لأن الشمس إذا وصلت إلى هذا الاعتدال ظهر في المركبات من أنواع النباتات نشو ونماء وبدا فيها مبادئ الثمار فهو أولى بالاعتبار إلى أن يتم الدور بما يليه من جانب الجنوب فصارت ثلاثة منها بين نقطتي الاعتدال الربيعي والانقلاب الصيفي، هي الحمل والثور والجوزاء وتسمى بروجاً ربيعية لأن الربيع في معظم المعمورة عبارة عن زمان كون الشمس فيها، وثلاثة منها بين الانقلاب الصيفي والاعتدال الخريفي هي السرطان والأسد والسنبلة وتسمى بروجاً صيفية لمث ما وثلاثة منها بين الاعتدال الخريفي والانقلاب الشتوي هي الميزان والعقرب والقوس وتسمى بروجاً خريفية وثلاثة منها بين الانقلاب الشتوي والاعتدال الربيعي وهي الجدي والدلو والحوت وتسمى بروجاً شتوية. قوله : ( فصارت إلخ) أي صارت دائرة ثالثة بعد المنطقتين.
And they began the consideration of the signs and the opening of the cycle with what follows the vernal equinox from the side of the north, because when the sun reaches this equinox, growth and development appear in composite things among the types of plants, and the beginnings of fruits appear in them, so it is more worthy of consideration, until the cycle is completed with what follows it from the side of the south. So three of them become between the two points of the vernal equinox and the summer solstice: they are Aries, Taurus, and Gemini, and they are called spring signs because spring in most of the inhabited world is the time when the sun is in them. And three of them between the summer solstice and the autumnal equinox: they are Cancer, Leo, and Virgo, and they are called summer signs likewise. And three of them between the autumnal equinox and the winter solstice: they are Libra, Scorpio, and Sagittarius, and they are called autumn signs. And three of them between the winter solstice and the vernal equinox: they are Capricorn, Aquarius, and Pisces, and they are called winter signs. His saying: 'so become (fārat) etc.' i.e., becomes a third circle after the two belts.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage explains the practical reasoning for the starting point of the zodiac cycle at the vernal equinox. The sun's arrival there triggers plant growth and the appearance of fruits, making it the logical beginning. The cycle proceeds through the seasons, resulting in four groups of three signs each: spring (Aries, Taurus, Gemini), summer (Cancer, Leo, Virgo), autumn (Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius), and winter (Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces). Each group corresponds to the sun's location during that season. The commentator notes that the phrase 'so become' refers to the formation of a third circle (the ecliptic?) after the two belts (the celestial equator and the zodiacal belt).

Key Terms
اعتدال ربيعي
انقلاب صيفي
اعتدال خريفي
انقلاب شتوي
بروج ربيعية
بروج صيفية
بروج خريفية
بروج شتوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seasonal names of the signs are based on actual weather patterns in a given region.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the seasons are defined by the sun's position relative to the equinoxes and solstices, not by temperature or weather. Spring, for example, is simply the time when the sun is in those signs, regardless of local climate.

Checkpoint
Which signs are classified as spring signs according to this passage?
A) Cancer, Leo, Virgo
B) Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius
C) Aries, Taurus, Gemini
D) Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces

Key Takeaway
The zodiac cycle is rooted in observable celestial events, with the vernal equinox as a natural starting point, and signs grouped into seasons based on the sun's position between equinoxes and solstices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author starts the zodiac at the moment when growth becomes visible. In your own life, what 'starting point' have you chosen that was grounded in observable change, rather than convenience? If you cannot think of one, what would it mean to begin something today based on clear evidence?
?
The passage links celestial observations to the appearance of fruits and growth. Are you attentive to the signs of Allah in the natural world around you, or have you become numb to them? What one practice could you start to restore that awareness?
?
The zodiac cycle is carefully ordered from the equinox. In your faith and learning, are you careful to order your priorities based on what is most foundational and observable, or do you jump into conclusions? What would change if you started from the clearest signs?

Tawali and Khilaf al-Tawali
Defines tawali and khilaf al-tawali with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two orders of the zodiac signs—tawali and khilaf al-tawali—and why tawali is considered the standard order.

Core Idea
The direction of planets' proper motions determines the standard order of signs.

This passage defines two key terms used in traditional Islamic astronomy: tawali and khilaf al-tawali. It explains the standard order of the zodiac signs based on the motion of the planets. Understanding these terms is essential for grasping how astronomers track planetary positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to identify the two terms being defined: tawali and khilaf al-tawali.
Notice that the passage first gives the definition, then the reasoning.
2
Identify the directions
Tawali is from west to east; khilaf al-tawali is from east to west. Commit these directions to memory.
The direction of tawali matches the planets' proper motions.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that tawali was chosen because the purpose is to regulate planetary motions, which are from west to east.
This reasoning ties the order directly to observed celestial motion.
4
Reflect on importance
Consider why distinguishing these two orders matters in astronomy. Which one is used for planetary tracking and why?
Tawali is the standard; khilaf al-tawali is its opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الترتيب الذي ذكرناه فيما بين البروج يسمى التوالي وهو من المغرب إلى المشرق وإنما اعتبروه كذلك إذ المقصود ضبط حركات الكواكب أعني حركاتها الخاصة وهي من المغرب إلى المشرق وعكسه يسمى خلاف التوالي وهو من المشرق إلى المغرب.
And this order which we have mentioned among the signs is called al-tawālī, and it is from the west to the east. They considered it thus only because the purpose is to regulate the motions of the planets—I mean their proper motions—which are from the west to the east. And its opposite is called khilāf al-tawālī, and it is from the east to the west.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This short passage defines two technical terms: al-tawālī (the order of the signs from west to east) and khilāf al-tawālī (the reverse order from east to west). The standard order (tawālī) corresponds to the direction of the planets' proper motions, which is from west to east. The reverse order is used for other purposes. This distinction is important for tracking planetary positions.

Misconception: The term 'tawālī' might be thought to mean any order of signs, but it specifically refers to the west-to-east order.
Correction: Tawālī is strictly defined as the order from west to east, based on the direction of planetary proper motions. The reverse is a separate term, khilāf al-tawālī.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Are you regulating your actions according to a deeper reality, or merely following habit? Commit to one change today that aligns your schedule with a meaningful purpose.

The Circle Passing Through the Four Poles
The author defines a great circle through the celestial and ecliptic poles and explains its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a special great circle that passes through the four poles of the celestial equator and the zodiac, and why it is geometrically necessary to pass through the points of maximum separation between the two belts.

Core Idea
A great circle through all four celestial poles is a necessary feature of the spherical model.

In this passage, the author introduces a specific great circle that passes through both pairs of poles: the celestial equator's poles and the ecliptic's poles. This circle is not arbitrary; it is geometrically required to also pass through the farthest points between the two celestial belts. Understanding this circle helps us visualize the relationship between the equatorial and ecliptic coordinate systems.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the main idea, then read the translation to ensure understanding.
2
Identify the four poles and two belts
Identify which poles are being referred to: the poles of the celestial equator (muʿaddil al-nahār) and the poles of the zodiac (falak al-burūj). Also identify the two belts: the celestial equator and the ecliptic.
3
Understand the necessity
The passage states that this circle must pass through the extreme distance between the two belts. Think about why this might be a geometric necessity in a spherical model.
The commentary provides a geometric justification using perpendicular circles, but it is cut off.
4
Review the alternative name
Note that the circle is also called simply 'al-mārra' (the passing one).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم توهموا دائرة مارة بالأقطاب الأربعة أعني قطبي معدل النهار وقطبي فلك البروج وسموها بهذا الاسم، ولا بد أن تمر هذه الدائرة بغاية البعد بين المنطقتين كما بين في الأكر. قوله : ( وسموها بهذا الاسم أي سموها بالدائرة المارة بالأقطاب الأربعة وقد يطلق عليها اسم المارة وحده كما ذكره بقوله : فإذا تقاطعت كذلك إلخ يعني أن كل دائرة إذا تقاطعت كذلك أي قاطعت على قوائم دائرتين وجب أن يكون قطباها موضع أيضاً أن تقاطعهما ووجب مارة بأقطابهما الأربعة وهاتان الدائرتان اللتان قاطعتهما هذه الدائرة كذلك، إما أن يكون هي تكونا متقاطعتين على قوائم كدائرة نصف النهار ودائرة أول السماوات فإنهما متقاطعتان على
Then they imagined a circle passing through the four poles—that is, the two poles of the celestial equator (muʿaddil al-nahār) and the two poles of the zodiac (falak al-burūj)—and they named it by this name. And this circle must pass through the extreme distance between the two belts (al-mintaqatayn), as is shown in the spheres (al-ukar). His saying: 'And they named it by this name' i.e., they named it 'the circle passing through the four poles', and it may be called simply 'the passing one' (al-mārra), as he mentioned in his saying: 'for when they intersect thus etc.' Meaning that every circle, when it intersects thus—i.e., cuts perpendicularly two circles—its poles must also be the point of intersection of those two circles, and it must pass through their four poles. And these two circles which this circle intersects are likewise, either they are intersecting perpendicularly, like the meridian circle and the circle of the first heavens, for they are perpendicularly intersecting
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage introduces a special great circle that passes through the four poles: the north and south poles of the celestial equator (muʿaddil al-nahār) and the north and south poles of the zodiac (falak al-burūj). The name given to it is 'the circle passing through the four poles' (al-dāʼira al-mārra bi-l-aqṭāb al-arbaʻa). The author asserts that this circle must also pass through the points of greatest separation (ghāyat al-buʻd) between the two belts (the celestial equator and the ecliptic). The commentator explains that this circle can also be called simply 'the passing one' (al-mārra). He then provides a geometric argument: if a circle cuts two other circles perpendicularly, then its poles must be the intersection points of those two circles, and it must pass through their poles. As examples, he mentions the meridian circle and the circle of the first heavens (probably the horizon or another reference), which are perpendicularly intersecting. The argument is cut off but is meant to justify the existence and necessity of this circle.

Key Terms
الأقطاب الأربعة
قطبا معدل النهار
قطبا فلك البروج
غاية البعد بين المنطقتين
دائرة المارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This circle is just any arbitrary circle on the celestial sphere.
Correction: It is geometrically necessary for the spherical model, as it must pass through the points of maximum distance between the two belts.

Checkpoint
Through which points must the circle passing through the four poles also pass?
A) The equinoctial points
B) The solstitial points
C) The points of maximum distance between the two belts
D) The centers of the signs

Key Takeaway
The circle passing through the four poles is not arbitrary; it is a necessary circle that passes through the points of maximum distance between the celestial equator and the ecliptic.

The Horizon Circle: Real and Sensory
Defines the horizon circle and distinguishes real vs sensory horizon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the horizon circle and the distinction between the real horizon (al-ufuq al-ḥaqīqī) and the sensory horizon (al-ufuq al-ḥissī).

Core Idea
The horizon is a geometric circle relative to your location, not just the visible line.

This passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif introduces the first astronomical circle – the horizon – and clarifies two distinct meanings: the real geometric horizon used in calculations, and the sensory visible horizon. Understanding this distinction is essential for following later discussions on the positions of celestial bodies and the directions of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its English translation together. Notice the technical terms for horizon, zenith, and nadir.
The passage begins with a subhead, then defines the horizon circle, then splits into two types.
2
Identify the two types of horizon
The author defines the real horizon first: a great circle whose poles are the zenith and nadir. Then he gives two definitions of the sensory horizon: one as a plane parallel to the real horizon, the other as a cone traced by an observer's line of sight.
The sensory horizon is relative to the observer, while the real horizon is fixed for a given location.
3
Recognize the importance of the real horizon
The author states that most astronomical rulings are based on the real horizon. This highlights its foundational role in calculations.
This explains why the distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - الفصل الثاني : القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : في اتجاهات الجسم
)
هذه الخمس
والنصف الخفي من الفلك وتسمى هذه الدائرة ( دائرة الأفق ) ولا شك أن الظهور والخفاء أمران بالإضافة إلى سكان بقعة من بقاع الأرض فيكون الأفق بملاحظة السفليات ) وتختلف بحسب اختلاف ( البقاع ) فإن كل بقعة على الأرض لها أفق على حده ) وقطباها سمت الرأس والقدم في تلك البقعة ( وأربعة ) من ( تمر بقطبيها ) أي بقطبي الأفق فتكون هي أيضاً بملاحظة السفليات .

قطباها سمت الرأس وسمت القدم وليست هي إلا الأفق الحقيقي. وأما الأفق الحسي فهي دائرة فاصلة بين ما يرى من یرى من الفلك وما لا يرى وتعبرت على وجهين أحدهما أن تكون هي منبسطة على وجه الأرض موازية للأفق الحسي بحيث يكون بعد ما بينهما مقدار نصف قطر الأرض، والثاني أن يفرض خط خارج من الناظر إلى موضع من الفلك كالمشرق مثلاً ثم يدار رأس ذلك الخط من ذلك الموضع إلى أن يعود إلى ذلك الموضع فيتخيل من حركة هذا الخط على هذا الوجه دائرة لا يتعين موضعها من الفلك بل ربما كانت منطبقة على الأفق الحقيقي وربما كانت واقعة فوقه وربما كانت واقعة تحته وكذا حاله بالنسبة إلى الأفق الحسي المذكور أولاً ثم لا يذهب عليك أن أكثر الأحكام المتعلقة بالأفق إنما هو مبني على الأفق الحقيقي كما ستنبه عليه
بإذن
الله تعالى
The first observation - the second chapter: the first section: the second intention: on the directions of the body. These five [circles?] and the hidden half of the sphere, and this circle is called the horizon circle. There is no doubt that visibility and invisibility are relative to the inhabitants of a particular spot on earth, so the horizon is considered with respect to lower things. And it differs according to the difference of places, for every spot on earth has its own horizon. Its poles are the zenith and nadir in that spot. And four [circles] pass through its poles, i.e., the poles of the horizon, so they are also considered with respect to lower things. Its poles are the zenith and nadir, and it is nothing but the real horizon. As for the sensory horizon, it is a circle separating what is seen of the sphere from what is not seen, and it is expressed in two ways: one is that it is a plane on the surface of the earth parallel to the sensory horizon such that the distance between them is half the earth's radius; the second is that a line is assumed extending from the observer to a point on the sphere, such as the east, then the head of that line is rotated from that point until it returns to that point, and from the motion of this line in this manner a circle is imagined whose position on the sphere is not fixed; rather it may coincide with the real horizon, or it may be above it, or below it. The same applies to the sensory horizon mentioned first. Then do not overlook that most of the rulings related to the horizon are based on the real horizon, as will be pointed out, by God's permission.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the first circle (the horizon) and distinguishes two types: the real horizon (al-ufuq al-ḥaqīqī) and the sensory horizon (al-ufuq al-ḥissī). The author begins by explaining that the horizon circle divides the sphere into a visible half and a hidden half, and that visibility is relative to the observer's location. The poles of the horizon are the zenith (samt al-ra's) and nadir (samt al-qadam) for that location. Then he explains the real horizon: it is a great circle whose plane passes through the center of the earth, and its poles are the zenith and nadir. The sensory horizon is defined in two ways: first, as a plane parallel to the real horizon at a distance of half the earth's radius (i.e., the visible horizon as a tangent plane); second, as an imaginary circle traced by a line from the observer to a point on the celestial sphere, which may coincide with, be above, or below the real horizon. The author emphasizes that most astronomical judgments rely on the real horizon.

Key Terms
الأفق الحقيقي
الأفق الحسي
سمت الرأس
سمت القدم
دائرة الأفق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking the visible horizon (sensory) is the same as the geometric horizon (real).
Correction: The real horizon is a perfect circle through the earth's center, while the sensory horizon depends on the observer's position and atmospheric conditions.

Checkpoint
What are the two types of horizon defined in this passage?
A) The northern and southern horizons
B) The real horizon (al-ufuq al-ḥaqīqī) and the sensory horizon (al-ufuq al-ḥissī)
C) The eastern and western horizons
D) The upper and lower horizons

Key Takeaway
The real horizon is the geometric circle used in astronomical calculations, while the sensory horizon is the apparent visible horizon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The real horizon is invisible yet foundational to accurate calculation. What foundational truths of your faith do you neglect because they are not immediately visible to your senses? Will you commit today to studying and acting on them even when they are not apparent?
?
Your sensory horizon shifts depending on your location and perspective. In what areas of your life—worship, relationships, priorities—are you letting your limited perspective define what is true instead of the unchangeable standard of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What step will you take this week to align your sight with the real horizon?
?
The author warns that most rulings depend on the real horizon, not the sensory one. Think of a recent situation where you acted based on what felt right or convenient rather than what is actually commanded. How can you train yourself to prioritize objective truth over subjective feeling from now on?

The Imaginary Nature of Astronomical Circles
Classifies astronomical circles and declares them imaginary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ten great astronomical circles are considered imaginary (mawhūma) and not subjects of belief in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The ten great circles are imaginary tools, not physical realities or articles of faith.

This passage from al-Mawaqif deals with astronomical concepts that were part of the scientific background of the time. However, the author immediately clarifies that these circles are not to be mistaken for real entities or matters of belief. He includes them only to explain the astronomers' purpose, not to establish any theological truth. As you read, notice how he balances technical description with a clear disclaimer about their ontological status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a sense of the technical terms and the flow.
The passage contains many specialized terms for different circles.
2
Identify the circles and classification
Notice that the author lists several circles, then classifies the last five into fixed and changing ones. Mark which are fixed and which change.
Fixed: horizon, meridian, first heaven. Changing: altitude circle, middle of visible sky.
3
Grasp the epistemological verdict
Pay attention to the concluding sentences where the author states that all these circles are imaginary (umūr mawhūma), have no external existence, are not matters of belief, and that he only included them to explain astronomers' aims.
This is the key doctrinal point: these are not real physical entities.

دائرة أول السماوات و) تنطبق هذه الدائرة ( عليها ) أي على أول السماوات ( إن كان الكوكب (عليها ) وحينئذ لم يكن للكوكب سمت كما عرفت وهذا الانطباق إنما يظهر إذا لم يكن الكوكب في احدى الغايتين. وأما إذا فرض أنه في إحداهما مع كونه على دائرة أول السماوات كما إذا كان على سمت الرأس أو القدم. فإنه يجوز اعتبار انطباقها على كل واحدة من نصف النهار وأول السماوات ( وهذه الدوائر الخمس الأخيرة وحدتها نوعية ولكل واحدة منها أشخاص كثيرة غير محصورة لكن ثلاث منها لا تتغير في كل بقعة ) بل كل واحدة منها لا تكون في بقعة واحدة متعددة بل شخصاً واحداً ( وهي دائرة الأفق ووسط السماء وأول السماوات واثنتان منها تتغيران ) في بقعة واحدة آناً فاناً وهي دائرة الارتفاع فإنها تتغير ( لحركة الكواكب ودائرة وسط سماء الرؤية ) فإنها تتغير ( لحركة قطبي منطقة البروج بتحريك المعدل لهما حول قطبيه بالحركة اليومية فهذه) الدوائر العشر العظام وغيرها وما يبتنى عليها ( أمور موهومة لا وجود لها في الخارج ولا (حجر) من جهة الشرع ( في مثلها ولا تتعلق باعتقاد ولا يتوجه نحوها إثبات وإبطال) فلم يكن بنا حاجة إلى ذكرها في كتابنا هذا إلا أنا أوردناها ) فيه (لتقف على مقصدهم في علم الهيئة ( وإذا رأيته محض تخيلات أوهن من بيت العنكبوت لم يهلك ) أي لم يفزعك ) سماع هذه الألفاظ ذوات القعاقع ) . القعقعة صوت السلاح ونحوه من الأمور اليابسة وفي المثل ما يقعقع لي بالشنان يعني أن هذه الألفاظ أصوات لا طائل تحتها كأصوات الأسلحة ونحوها من الجمادات هذا ما ذكره.
The First Observatory - Chapter Two: The First Section: The Second Intention: On the Directions of the Body. Planets (many) visible, so it is the visible sky, and this circle is in its center. (And the fifth:) (from them) passes through the poles of the horizon and through a certain planet, i.e., through the endpoint of a line extending from the center of the world to the surface of the sphere passing through its center, and it is called the circle of altitude and declination, since an arc of it (lying) between the horizon and the planet on the eastern side is its altitude, on the western side its declination. The correct view is that the first arc is its eastern altitude, the second its western altitude. As for declination, it is an arc of it below the horizon, either on the western or eastern side. The arc lying on the horizon between the circle of altitude and one of the two points of east and west is called the azimuth. So when the circle of the planet's altitude coincides with the circle of the first heaven, it has no azimuth arc because it then passes through the two points of east and west. (And this circle, at the planet's maximum altitude, coincides with the circle of the middle of the sky) i.e., the meridian; likewise at its maximum declination. So in every revolution by the first motion, the altitude circle coincides with the meridian twice. Its coincidence with it only occurs if the planet is not on the circle of the first heaven. And this circle (coincides) with it, i.e., with the first heaven, if the planet is on it, and then the planet has no azimuth as you know. This coincidence only appears if the planet is not at one of the two extremes. But if it is assumed to be at one of them while being on the circle of the first heaven—as when it is at the zenith or nadir—then it is permissible to consider its coincidence with each of the meridian and the first heaven. (And these last five circles: their unity is kind, and each has many unenumerable individuals, but three of them do not change in every locality) rather each of them is not multiple in one locality but is one individual: they are the horizon circle, the middle of the sky, and the first heaven. Two of them change in a single locality moment by moment: the altitude circle, for it changes due to the motion of the planets; and the circle of the middle of the visible sky, for it changes due to the motion of the poles of the zodiac by the equinoctial moving them around its poles with the daily motion. So these are the ten great circles and others and what is built upon them: imaginary things with no existence in the external world. There is no restriction from the Law regarding such matters, they are not connected with belief, and no proof or refutation is directed toward them. So we had no need to mention them in this our book, except that we have included them to acquaint you with their aim in the science of astronomy. (And if you see it as pure imaginations weaker than a spider's web, do not be alarmed), i.e., do not be frightened by hearing these loud-sounding words. 'Qaqa'a' is the sound of weapons and similar dry things. In the proverb: 'What rattles my water-skins?' meaning these words are sounds with no substance beneath them, like the sounds of weapons and similar lifeless things. That is what he mentioned.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a technical description of astronomical circles followed by an epistemological clarification. The author first defines several celestial circles: the horizon, the meridian (middle of sky), the first heaven (probably the celestial equator), and the altitude/declination circle. He explains how arcs relate to altitude and azimuth, and how circles coincide under certain conditions. He then classifies five final circles: three are fixed (horizon, meridian, first heaven) and two change (altitude circle and the middle of the visible sky). All these circles are part of the 'ten great circles' of astronomy. The author then makes a crucial doctrinal point: these circles are 'imaginary things' (umūr mawhūma) with no external existence. They are not regulated by Islamic law, not matters of belief, and no proof or refutation is directed at them. He states that he included them in his book only to explain the astronomers' purpose. He warns readers not to be alarmed by the grand-sounding terminology, which is like empty noises. This guards against the misconception that such technical details are essential to faith.

Correction: The author explicitly states they are imaginary things (umūr mawhūma) with no external existence, not subject to belief or proof.

Objection on the Reality of Circles and the Difficulty of Observation
Objection that spherical motion requires real circles, and reply on observation difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that spherical motion entails real circles and the response that precise astronomical observation is extremely difficult, leading to approximate knowledge.

Core Idea
Spherical motion implies circles, but exact knowledge is beyond human precision.

This passage presents a key objection against the idea that celestial circles are merely imaginary. The objector argues that spheres moving on their centers necessitate real poles and circles. The commentator responds by acknowledging the logical necessity but emphasizing the extreme difficulty of accurate observation, showing that our knowledge of such motions is only approximate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part where the objector argues that a moving sphere must have stationary poles, an equinoctial circle, and smaller parallel circles. Notice the logical necessity of these geometric features.
2
Understand the logical necessity
Reflect on why the objector claims these circles are real: they are inherent to the motion itself, not merely imagined. This challenges the idea that they are only theoretical constructs.
3
Read the response on observation difficulty
Read the commentator's reply that precise regulation of planetary motions depends on flawless instruments and careful observation, which is practically impossible. This shows why actual astronomical data differ.
4
Note the conclusion
Understand that the commentator does not deny the logical necessity but argues that certain knowledge is unattainable; we have only approximations. This reconciles the objection with empirical reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : لا شك أن الكرة إذا تحركت على مركزها من غير أن تخرج عن مكانها فلا بد أن ينفرض فيها نقطتان لا حركة لهما أصلاً وهما القطبان وأن ينفرض فيما بينهما دائرة عظيمة هي في حلق الوسط بينهما وتكون الحركة عليها سريعة وهي المنطقة وأن ينفرض من جنبتيها دوائر صغار موازية لها تكون الحركة عليها بطيئة بالقياس إليها بطئاً متفاوتاً جداً. فما هو أقرب إلى القطب يكون أبطأ مما هو أقرب إلى المنطقة ولا شبهة أيضاً في أن الكرات إذا أحاط بعضها ببعض أمكن أن تكون حركاتها بحيث تتقاطع مناطقها إذا اعتبرت في كرة واحدة منها وحينئذ ينفرض هناك بين المنطقتين نقطتا تقاطع ونقطتا غاية البعد بينهما، فهذه وأمثالها وإن لم تكن من الحساب ... قوله : ( ولقائل أن يقول إلخ ما ذكره قدس سره حتى إلى قوله ينضبط بهذه الأمور أحوال الحركات فإن ضبطها موقوف على صنعة الآلات الرصدية من غير غلط ونصبها في دائرة نصف النهار تحقيقاً . وإحساس الكواكب عند وصولها بتدقيق النظر غفلة وعدم الخلط في واجتماع هذه الأمور على التحقيق متعسر بل متعذر ولذا اختلف الإرصاد في ضبط حركة الكواكب سيما حركة عطارد فإنها ليست متشابهة لا عند مركز العالم ولا عند خارج مركزه وضبط الإقبال والإدبار ، نعم إنهم بذلوا الوسع في ضبطها بقدر الإمكان وأما الاطلاع عليها على ما هو في نفس الأمر فكلا . بناء على أن سمت رأس هذا الشخص وسمت قدمه يغاير سمت رأس الشخص الآخر وسمت قدمه فلا اعتبار لذلك القدر من التعدد. قوله : (لم يفزعك ) الإفزاع الإخافة. وقوله : بالشنان الشن بفتح الشين المعجمة وتشديد النون القربة الخلق والجمع الشنان وقوله : من يزدريها أي من يستحقرها .
And someone may say: There is no doubt that when a sphere moves on its center without leaving its place, it is necessary to suppose in it two points that have no motion at all, namely the poles, and to suppose between them a great circle that is in the middle between them, and motion on it is swift—that is the equinoctial—and to suppose on its two sides small circles parallel to it, motion on which is slow relative to it, with very varying slowness. For what is closer to the pole is slower than what is closer to the equinoctial. There is also no doubt that when spheres surround one another, it is possible that their motions be such that their equinoctials intersect when considered in one sphere, and then there are supposed between the two equinoctials two points of intersection and two points of maximum distance. These and the like, even if not from calculation... His words: (And someone may say etc.) What he (may his soul be sanctified) mentioned up to his saying: 'the states of motions are regulated by these matters.' For their regulation depends on the manufacture of observational instruments without error, and their installation in the meridian circle accurately. And sensing the planets at their arrival with precise observation, lack of oversight, and no confusion—gathering these conditions for accuracy is difficult, even impossible. Hence observations have differed in regulating the motion of the planets, especially the motion of Mercury, for it is not uniform either at the center of the world or at its eccentric. And regulation of direct and retrograde motion... Yes, they exerted effort to regulate it as much as possible, but as for knowing it as it is in itself—no. Based on that the zenith of this person and his nadir differ from the zenith of another person and his nadir, so that degree of multiplicity is not considered. His words: (do not be alarmed) 'Ifza' means fear. His words: 'with the water-skins'—'shinn' (with opened shin and doubled noon) means old water-skin, plural 'shinan'. His words: 'who despises them' means who holds them in contempt.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a response. The objector argues that spherical motion necessarily implies the existence of real poles and circles (equinoctial, small circles parallel to it, intersections). These are not imaginary but geometrically necessary. The commentator responds by agreeing that such things are conceptually required but then argues that precise determination of these circles and planetary motions is extremely difficult. Accurate observation requires flawless instruments, exact placement in the meridian, careful sensing without error—conditions that are practically unattainable. This explains why observations of planetary motions (especially Mercury) differ. Astronomers can only approximate, not achieve certain knowledge of things as they are in themselves. The commentator also notes that the zenith varies per person, so no fixed reality attaches to these imagined circles. Thus the objection is answered: while the concepts are logically necessary, their precise realization in external reality is beyond human grasp, so they remain 'imaginary' for practical purposes. The passage ends with lexical explanations of earlier terms (ifza', shinn, yazdariha).

Are the Celestial Circles Real or Imaginary?
Al-Jurjani declares the ten great circles imaginary and dismisses their theological importance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani considers the ten great celestial circles to be imaginary and not matters of religious belief.

Core Idea
The ten great circles are imaginary tools, not real existents.

In this passage, al-Jurjani makes a bold epistemological claim: the circles that astronomers discuss—such as the equinoctial and ecliptic—have no external reality. They are useful fictions for calculation, but not objects of faith or law. He even mocks those who are overly impressed by the technical jargon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on imaginary circles
Start by reading the Arabic and translation where al-Jurjani states that the ten great circles are 'amoor mawhooma' (imaginary matters) and that they have no existence outside the mind.
2
Notice the three reasons for their irrelevance
Identify his three points: no legal restriction, no connection to belief, and no need for affirmation or negation.
This shows they are not part of theology.
3
Understand the 'al-qa'qa'a' proverb
He uses a proverb to compare the technical terms to empty noises. Reflect on why he uses such strong language.
He is warning against being awed by sound without substance.
4
Connect to the author's purpose
Despite their unreality, he mentions them to explain astronomers' aims. Ask yourself why a theologian would include this.
This bridges kalam and astronomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السماء وأول السماوات واثنتان منها تتغيران في بقعة واحدة آناً فاناً وهي دائرة الارتفاع فإنها تتغير لحركة الكواكب ودائرة وسط سماء الرؤية فإنها تتغير لحركة قطبي منطقة البروج بتحريك المعدل لهما حول قطبيه بالحركة اليومية فهذه الدوائر العشر العظام وغيرها وما يبتنى عليها أمور موهومة لا وجود لها في الخارج ولا حجر من جهة الشرع في مثلها ولا تتعلق باعتقاد ولا يتوجه نحوها إثبات وإبطال فلم يكن بنا حاجة إلى ذكرها في كتابنا هذا إلا أنا أوردناها فيه لتقف على مقصدهم في علم الهيئة وإذا رأيته محض تخيلات أوهن من بيت العنكبوت لم يهلك أي لم يفزعك سماع هذه الألفاظ ذوات القعاقع. القعقعة صوت السلاح ونحوه من الأمور اليابسة وفي المثل ما يقعقع لي بالشنان يعني أن هذه الألفاظ أصوات لا طائل تحتها كأصوات الأسلحة ونحوها من الجمادات هذا ما ذكره. ولقائل أن يقول: لا شك أن الكرة إذا تحركت على مركزها من غير أن تخرج عن مكانها فلا بد أن ينفرض فيها نقطتان لا حركة لهما أصلاً وهما القطبان وأن ينفرض فيما بينهما دائرة عظيمة هي في حلق الوسط بينهما وتكون الحركة عليها سريعة وهي المنطقة وأن ينفرض من جنبتيها دوائر صغار موازية لها تكون الحركة عليها بطيئة بالقياس إليها بطئاً متفاوتاً جداً. فما هو أقرب إلى القطب يكون أبطأ مما هو أقرب إلى المنطقة ولا شبهة أيضاً في أن الكرات إذا أحاط بعضها ببعض أمكن أن تكون حركاتها بحيث تتقاطع مناطقها إذا اعتبرت في كرة واحدة منها وحينئذ ينفرض هناك بين المنطقتين نقطتا تقاطع ونقطتا غاية البعد بينهما، فهذه وأمثالها وإن لم تكن من الحساب فإن ضبطها موقوف على صنعة الآلات الرصدية من غير غلط ونصبها في دائرة نصف النهار تحقيقاً. وإحساس الكواكب عند وصولها بتدقيق النظر غفلة وعدم الخلط في واجتماع هذه الأمور على التحقيق متعسر بل متعذر ولذا اختلف الإرصاد في ضبط حركة الكواكب سيما حركة عطارد فإنها ليست متشابهة لا عند مركز العالم ولا عند خارج مركزه وضبط الإقبال والإدبار، نعم إنهم بذلوا الوسع في ضبطها بقدر الإمكان وأما الاطلاع عليها على ما هو في نفس الأمر فكلا.
The sky and the first of the heavens, and two of them change in one spot at an instant, namely the circle of altitude, for it changes due to the motion of the planets, and the circle of the mid-heaven of sight, for it changes due to the motion of the poles of the ecliptic by the equinoctial moving them around its poles with the daily motion. So these ten great circles and others and what is built upon them are imaginary matters having no existence in external reality, and there is no restriction from the Law concerning them, nor are they related to belief, nor does affirmation or negation apply to them. So we had no need to mention them in this book of ours, except that we have mentioned them to acquaint you with their purpose in the science of astronomy. And if you see that they are mere delusions weaker than a spider's web, let it not frighten you to hear these resounding words. 'al-qa'qa'a' is the sound of weapons and the like of dry matters; in the proverb 'ma yuqa'qa' li bi al-shinān' meaning that these words are sounds with no substance beneath them, like the sounds of weapons and the like of inanimate objects. This is what he mentioned. And someone may say: There is no doubt that if a sphere moves around its center without leaving its place, then necessarily two points are assumed therein which have no motion at all, namely the two poles, and between them is assumed a great circle that is in the middle between them, and motion upon it is fast, which is the equator, and on its sides are assumed small circles parallel to it, motion upon which is slow in comparison to it, with very varying slowness. So that which is closer to the pole is slower than that which is closer to the equator. And there is no doubt also that if spheres surround each other, it is possible that their motions are such that their equators intersect when considered in one of them, and then there are assumed between the two equators two points of intersection and two points of greatest distance between them. So these and the like, even if they are not from calculation, their accurate determination depends on the manufacture of observational instruments without error and their placement in the meridian circle accurately. And the sensing of the planets when they arrive by precise observation is [subject to] inattention and error in combination, and the gathering of these matters with accuracy is difficult, nay impossible. Hence the observations differed in determining the motion of the planets, especially the motion of Mercury, for it is not uniform, neither at the center of the world nor outside its center, and the determination of its advance and retreat. Yes, they exerted effort to determine it as much as possible, but to know it as it is in itself, no.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument that begins by stating that the ten great celestial circles (such as the equinoctial, ecliptic, etc.) are imaginary (موهومة) and have no external existence. The author says they were mentioned only to explain the aims of astronomers, but they are not matters of belief or legal concern. He scoffs at those who are awed by the technical terms, comparing them to empty noises. Then he raises an objection from a hypothetical questioner: given the geometry of spheres in motion, certain points and circles (poles, equator, parallels, intersection points) are geometrically necessary; therefore these entities must be real, not imaginary. The commentator responds by shifting the debate from theoretical geometry to practical astronomy. He argues that while these mathematical constructs are theoretically sound, their precise observational determination is extremely difficult. Accurate instruments, perfect alignment, and inaltentive observations lead to discrepancies. He mentions that even the best astronomers have disagreed on the motions of planets, especially Mercury. Thus, the circles remain useful theoretical fictions, not mind-independent realities. The commentator concludes by acknowledging that astronomers have done their best, but perfect knowledge of the true motions is unattainable.

أمور موهومة
القعقعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that because astronomers use these circles, they must be physically real and part of Islamic belief.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly denies external existence and any connection to belief or law; they are useful fictions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The ten great circles are imaginary constructs with no external reality and no religious significance, introduced only to explain astronomical aims.

Al-Jurjani warns against being 'frightened' by resounding words. In your own life, what intellectual or spiritual 'clangers' have intimidated you into neglecting core beliefs? Will you now identify one such distraction and consciously prioritize what has real substance for your faith?
?
If these imaginary circles have no religious significance, why do you spend time studying things that ultimately do not bring you closer to Allah? Examine your current pursuits: are they rooted in obedience and conviction, or are you chasing hollow sounds for pride or curiosity?
