The Commentator's Methodological Approach
Commentator praises author's reference but criticizes excessive detail, explaining his own brevity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator chooses brevity and how he evaluates the original author's work.

Core Idea
Less is more: The commentator favors concise references over verbose exposition.

The commentator begins by acknowledging the strength of the original author's reference technique, but then offers a critical observation about its application. He argues that excessive detail actually hinders understanding, which is why he himself adopts a more succinct style. This passage reveals the commentator's own methodological priority: clarity through conciseness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: praise, critique, then rationale for brevity.
The terms 'الحوالة' and 'الإطناب' are key.
2
Identify the two-part evaluation
The commentator first praises the author's reference ('أحسن') then suggests it would be better if generalized ('لو عممها'). Understand why he thinks the current approach causes difficulty.
The critique is about the manner of presentation, not the content.
3
Understand the commentator's own choice
He states he avoids verbosity ('الإطناب') and limits himself to what is in the book. This is his methodological stance for the commentary.
4
Reflect on the implication for studying the commentary
The commentator is signaling that his commentary will be concise and focused, avoiding extraneous discussions. This helps the reader know what to expect.
This passage sets the tone for the entire commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد أحسن في هذه الحوالة ولو عممها في أكثر المباحث السالفة وترك تفاصيلها لكان أحس وأحسن. لأن التعرض لها على الوجه الذي أورده أوجب انتشار الكلام وصعوبة الفهم، وتذييلها بمباحث أخرى يوجب زيادة في الصعوبة فلذلك أعرضنا عن الإطناب واقتصرنا على ما ذكر في الكتاب والله الموفق للصواب.
He did well in this reference, and if he had generalized it to most of the previous discussions and left their details, it would have been better and best. Because addressing them in the manner he presented necessitated the spread of speech and difficulty of understanding, and appending other discussions to them necessitates an increase in difficulty. Therefore we refrained from verbosity and limited ourselves to what is mentioned in the book, and God is the grantor of success to what is correct.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a methodological comment by the commentator (al-Sharīf al-Jurjānī) on the approach of the original author (al-Ījī). The commentator praises the author for making a reference (ḥawāla) to other works, but suggests that if the author had extended this practice to most earlier topics and omitted their details, it would have been even better. The reason is that delving into those details in the original work caused the discourse to become spread out and difficult to understand; adding further discussions only compounds the difficulty. Therefore, the commentator states that he himself—in his commentary—avoids verbosity (iṭnāb) and confines himself to what is in the book (i.e., the original text), trusting in God for guidance to what is correct.

Key Terms
الحوالة (al-ḥawāla)
الإطناب (al-iṭnāb)
المباحث (al-mabāḥith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the commentator is disparaging the author's work.
Correction: The commentator is not criticizing the content but rather explaining his own editorial choice to be concise. He acknowledges the author's good reference.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the author's practice of providing details?
A) It made the work easier to understand.
B) It caused the discourse to spread and become difficult to understand.
C) It was entirely without benefit.
D) It should have been applied to all topics.

Key Takeaway
The commentator prioritizes clarity and brevity, building upon the author's work while streamlining it for better understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a complex topic, do you prefer a source that covers every detail or one that focuses on the essentials? Which approach actually helps you understand better? Be honest: is your preference driven by intellectual laziness or genuine desire for clarity? How will you adjust your study habits after learning from the commentator's wisdom?
?
The commentator avoided verbosity because it 'spreads speech and makes understanding difficult.' Think of a time when you tried to explain something but ended up confusing yourself and others. What caused that? How can you intentionally practice conciseness in your own speech and teaching, even when you know more details?
?
The commentator still respects the author while choosing a different method. Is your respect for scholars sometimes hindered when you disagree with their style or approach? Can you separate the value of their content from your personal preferences? Commit to one practical change in how you engage with scholarly works that differ from your own style.

Cause of Lunar Eclipse
Defines lunar eclipse and explains the conical shadow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a lunar eclipse occurs and why Earth's shadow is conical.

Core Idea
Lunar eclipse is simply Earth blocking sunlight from reaching the moon.

The passage begins by defining a lunar eclipse: when the moon is opposite the sun near the nodes, Earth lies between them and blocks sunlight, causing the moon to appear dim. It then explains that because Earth is much smaller than the Sun, its shadow forms a cone, with its base on Earth and apex in space.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and cause
Read the Arabic passage describing what a lunar eclipse is and how Earth’s shadow causes it. Note the key terms: 'خسوف القمر' and 'مقابلة'.
Observe that the moon's dimness is due to lack of sunlight, not a change in the moon itself.
2
Understand the conical shadow
The passage states that the shadow is conical because the Earth is smaller than the Sun. Picture the geometry: Earth casts a cone of shadow into space.
The base of the cone is a small circle on Earth; the apex points away from the Sun.
3
Reflect on the explanation
Consider why the moon appears dim: its natural color is dark, and without sunlight it becomes visible. The passage clarifies this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني : خسوف القمر
في خسوف القمر وهو أنه قد يكون القمر مقابلا للشمس بقرب العقدتين فتكون الأرض حينئذ واقعة بينه وبين الشمس فتمنع الأرض ضوءها عنه فيرى كمداً كما هو لونه الأصلي ولأن جرم الأرض أصغر كثيراً من جرم الشمس فيقع الظل الناشئ من الأرض مخروطاً قاعدته دائرة صغيرة على الأرض ورأسه على محاذاة جزء من أجزاء فلك البروج مقابل لجزء منه حل فيه الشمس فإن لم يكن للقمر في حال المقابلة عرض بأن يكون في أحد العقدتين انخسف بالكلية لأنه أصغر من الأرض بل من غلظ الظل حيث وصل إليه فيقع كله في داخله ومكث فيه زماناً وإن كان له عرض فإن كان ذلك العرض بقدر نصف قطر صفحة القمر ونصف قطر دائرة الظل
قوله : (قاعدته) أي قاعدة الظل الناشئ من الأرض. وقوله : دائرة صغيرة وهذه الدائرة الصغيرة موازية للدائرة العظيمة منتصفة لكرة الأرض لكنها أصغر منها وذلك لأن الشمس أكبر بكثير من جرم الأرض. فلم تكن تلك الدائرة العظيمة منشأ للظل لوقوع ضوء الشمس على محيط تلك الدائرة العظيمة أيضاً. فكيف تكون هي منشأ للظل مع إحاطة الضوء عليها بل دائرة التي تكون أصغر من تلك الدائرة لكن هذه الدائرة التي تكون منشأ للظل لا بد أن تكون موازية لتلك الدائرة العظيمة. فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون قاعدة المخروطة دائرة صغيرة أيضاً كما ذكره . وقوله : على محاذاة جزء إلخ. يعني أنه يعتبر في فلك البروج جزءان متقابلان أحدهما ما حل فيه الشمس والآخر ما يكون محاذياً لرأس ذلك المخروط . قوله : بقدر نصف قطر صحيفة القمر وإنما لم يعتبر تمام كل واحد من القمر ودائرة المخروط لأن المعتبر هاهنا هو ميل مركز القمر عن مركز دائرة المخروط . فإذا مال مركز القمر عن مزكز دائرة المخروط بمقدار نصف قطره ونصف قطرها معا لم يبق بينه وبين الشمس حائل من الأرض أصلاً وقوله : خارجاً الضمير المستتر فيه راجع إلى سطح جرم القمر فهو إما أن يكون حالاً منه وإما أن يكون مفعولاً ثانياً للتوهم المذكور . أي من تخيل سطح جرم القمر سطحاً مستديراً
The Second Objective: Lunar Eclipse
Concerning lunar eclipse: it is that sometimes the moon is opposite the sun near the two nodes, then the earth lies between it and the sun, and the earth prevents the sun's light from reaching it, so it appears dim as its original color. And because the body of the earth is much smaller than the body of the sun, the shadow arising from the earth falls as a cone, its base a small circle on the earth, and its apex aligned with a part of the ecliptic opposite a part of it in which the sun resides. If the moon at opposition has no latitude—i.e., it is at one of the two nodes—it is totally eclipsed because it is smaller than the earth, indeed smaller than the thickness of the shadow where it reaches it, so it all falls inside it and remains in it for a time. And if it has latitude, then if that latitude is equal to half the radius of the moon's disk and half the radius of the shadow's circle...
His saying: 'Its base': meaning the base of the shadow arising from the earth. And his saying: 'a small circle': this small circle is parallel to the great circle that bisects the sphere of the earth, but it is smaller than it, and that is because the sun is much larger than the body of the earth. So that great circle is not the source of the shadow because sunlight also falls on the circumference of that great circle. How could it be the source of the shadow when light surrounds it? Rather, the circle that is smaller than that circle—but this circle that is the source of the shadow must be parallel to that great circle. Hence it follows that the base of the cone is a small circle, as he mentioned. And his saying: 'aligned with a part, etc.': meaning that in the ecliptic two opposite parts are considered, one of which is where the sun resides, and the other is aligned with the apex of that cone. His saying: 'equal to half the radius of the moon's disk': he did not consider the whole of each of the moon and the cone's circle because what is considered here is the deviation of the moon's center from the center of the cone's circle. So if the moon's center deviates from the center of the cone's circle by the amount of its own radius and the radius of that circle together, there remains no barrier from the earth between it and the sun at all. And his saying: 'outwardly'—the hidden pronoun refers to the surface of the moon's body, so it is either a state from it or a second object for the aforementioned supposition, i.e., from imagining the surface of the moon's body as a round surface.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines the cause of lunar eclipse and the condition for total eclipse. It begins by stating that lunar eclipse occurs when the moon is opposite the sun near the nodes (the intersection points of the moon's orbit with the ecliptic), and the earth is between them, blocking sunlight. The moon appears dim because its natural color is dark. The earth's shadow is conical because the earth is smaller than the sun; the base of the cone is a small circle on the earth, and the apex points away from the sun. If the moon is exactly at a node (no latitude), it is totally eclipsed because it is smaller than the shadow's thickness at that point, so it falls entirely within the shadow and remains there. If the moon has latitude, then the extent of eclipse depends on the size of that latitude relative to the radii of the moon and the shadow. The commentary then explains the geometry: the base of the shadow cone is a small circle parallel to the great circle that bisects the earth, because the sun is so large that its light surrounds the earth, only a smaller region produces a shadow. The alignment of the apex with a point in the ecliptic opposite the sun is clarified. The commentary also explains why only half the radii are considered: the critical factor is the distance between the centers of the moon and the shadow's cross-section; if that distance equals the sum of their radii, the moon just touches the shadow externally (no eclipse); if less, an eclipse occurs.

Key Terms
خسوف القمر
مقابلة
عقدتان
مخروط الظل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The moon changes color on its own during an eclipse.
Correction: The passage clarifies the moon appears dim because Earth blocks sunlight; the moon's natural color is dark.

Checkpoint
What causes the moon to appear dim during a lunar eclipse?
A) The moon moves into Earth's shadow.
B) The moon changes color.
C) The sun's light is blocked by the Earth.
D) The moon's own light fades.

Key Takeaway
Lunar eclipse occurs when Earth blocks sunlight from reaching the moon, causing it to appear dim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a lunar eclipse, do you simply marvel at its beauty, or does it prompt you to reflect on the precise order Allah has placed in the heavens? What steps can you take to make such natural phenomena a means of increasing your faith?
?
The moon's dimness is not a change in its own nature but a temporary absence of light. In your life, when you feel overwhelmed by 'shadows' or trials, do you remember that your true fitrah is still intact, and the darkness is merely an external blockage? How can you actively seek the light of guidance when obstacles appear?
?
This passage explains that lunar eclipses occur only when specific conditions are met—opposition and proximity to the nodes. How does this precise arrangement of celestial bodies stir within you a sense of awe and accountability to the One who set these laws? What specific act of gratitude or worship can you perform the next time you see a lunar eclipse?

Condition for Total Lunar Eclipse
States the condition for total lunar eclipse and the role of lunar latitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a total lunar eclipse requires the moon to have no latitude (be at a node) and how partial eclipses occur.

Core Idea
Total eclipse requires the moon to be exactly in the line of Earth's shadow.

After explaining the general cause, the passage now details the specific conditions for a total eclipse. It states that if the moon has no latitude—meaning it is exactly at one of the nodes—then it will be completely engulfed by Earth's shadow. If it has latitude, the eclipse will be partial, and the extent depends on the size of that latitude relative to the radii of the moon and the shadow.

Guided Path
1
Read the total eclipse condition
Read the sentence: 'If the moon at opposition has no latitude—i.e., it is at one of the two nodes—it is totally eclipsed.' Notice the reason given: it is smaller than the shadow's thickness.
The term 'عرض' (latitude) refers to the moon's angular distance from the ecliptic plane.
2
Understand the partial eclipse scenario
The passage then introduces the case when the moon has latitude. It gives a specific measurement: if the latitude equals the sum of half the moon's radius and half the shadow's radius, the moon just touches the shadow externally.
This geometry determines whether and how much the moon enters the shadow.
3
Visualize the geometry
The commentary explains the 'sum of half radii' condition: it is the distance between the centers of the moon and the shadow's cross-section. When that distance equals the sum of the two radii, no eclipse occurs; when less, a partial or total eclipse happens.
This is a standard geometric condition for two circles touching externally.

Conditions for No Eclipse, Partial Eclipse, and Total Eclipse with Duration
Enumerates three eclipse conditions based on lunar latitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three geometric conditions for lunar eclipses: no eclipse, partial eclipse, and total eclipse with or without duration.

Core Idea
The moon's latitude relative to the shadow determines eclipse type and duration.

This passage explores the precise geometrical relationships between the moon's orbital latitude and the size of the Earth's shadow cone. By comparing the moon's distance from the ecliptic to the combined radii of the moon and the shadow's cross-section, we can predict whether an eclipse occurs and its nature. The author presents three distinct cases that every student of eclipse astronomy must internalize.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the full literal translation. Notice how the author enumerates each case.
Pay attention to the terms 'latitude' ('ard) and 'sum of the two halves' (majmu' al-nisfayn).
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage describes three outcomes: no eclipse when latitude equals sum of halves, partial when latitude is less than sum, and total when latitude equals or is less than the excess.
Note the sub-condition for total eclipse with or without duration.
3
Understand the 'no duration' case
When latitude exactly equals the excess of shadow's radius over moon's radius, the moon just touches the inner shadow, giving a total eclipse but with no duration.
This is a subtle but important distinction.
4
Connect to the commentary
Read the commentary (from qalu) to clarify the definitions of 'circle of shadow' and the geometrical reasoning behind the conditions.
The commentary also explains the term 'excess' as the difference in radii.
5
Test understanding with checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to verify you can apply the condition for total eclipse with duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصف قطر) دائرة ( الظل ) وهي الدائرة الحادثة على مخروط الظل من توهم سطح جرم القمر الذي يرى كدائرة خارجياً إلى أن ينقطع المخروط (لم ينخسف ) القمر حينئذ بل ماس الظل من خارج كحدبتي دائرتين ( وإن كان ( ذلك العرض ( أقل ) من مجموع النصفين المذكورين انخسف بعضه وذلك بقدر تقاطع القطرين) أي تلاقيهما وتداخلهما فإن فرض أن هذا العرض الأقل يساوي فضل نصف قطر الظل على نصف قطر القمر انخسف كله وماس سطحه دائرة الظل من داخل ولم يكن له مكث وإن كان أقل من ذلك الفضل انخسف بتمامه ومكث بحسب وقوعه في الظل.
قوله : ( وهي الدائرة إلخ ) يعني أن سطح جرم القمر وإن كان لا يرى في الاستقبال في البعد العادة كدائرة القمر ويسمى صفة القمر فإذا خرج ذلك السطح في الوهم إلى أن يقع هناك مخروط الظل، وحدث في المخروط دائرة موازية لقاعدته يسمى دائرة الظل يكون مركزها أيضاً على المنطقة . قوله : ( يساوي فضل إلخ ) أو النصف قطر القمر على ما بين في محله. قوله: انخسف كله لوقوعه بتمامه تحت الظل . قوله : (لم يكن له مكث بل تبدى في الحال بالانجلاء بعد زيادة الظل على جرم القمر.
And half the radius of the circle of the shadow—which is the circle occurring on the cone of the shadow from imagining the surface of the moon's body that is seen as a circle outwardly until the cone ends—(the moon is not eclipsed then, but rather touches the shadow from outside like the convexities of two circles). And if that latitude is less than the sum of the two mentioned halves, part of it is eclipsed, and that is by the amount of the intersection of the two diameters, i.e., their meeting and interpenetration. For if we suppose that this lesser latitude equals the excess of the radius of the shadow over the radius of the moon, it is totally eclipsed, and its surface touches the circle of the shadow from inside, and it has no duration. And if it is less than that excess, it is completely eclipsed and has duration according to its falling within the shadow.
His saying: 'which is the circle, etc.': meaning that the surface of the moon's body, even though it is not seen in opposition at the usual distance as the circle of the moon and is called the 'disk of the moon', so if that surface is taken in imagination until the cone of the shadow occurs there, and there occurs in the cone a circle parallel to its base called the 'circle of the shadow', its center is also on the ecliptic. His saying: 'equals the excess, etc.': or the radius of the moon, as explained in its place. His saying: 'it is totally eclipsed': because it falls entirely under the shadow. His saying: 'it has no duration': rather it appears immediately after the shadow increases upon the moon's body.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage continues the lunar eclipse discussion, presenting the conditions for no eclipse, partial eclipse, and total eclipse with duration. It begins by considering the case when the moon's latitude equals the sum of half the radius of the moon and half the radius of the shadow's cross-section: the moon just touches the shadow externally, resulting in no eclipse. If the latitude is less than that sum, a partial eclipse occurs, with the amount determined by the overlap of the two circles (moon and shadow cross-section). If the latitude equals the excess of the shadow's radius over the moon's radius, the entire moon enters the shadow but only touches internally, so it is totally eclipsed but with no duration (immediately starts to emerge). If the latitude is even smaller, the moon is completely immersed in the shadow, causing a total eclipse with duration. The commentary clarifies that the 'circle of the shadow' is an imaginary cross-section parallel to the base, centered on the ecliptic. It also explains the terms: 'excess' refers to the difference in radii, and 'no duration' means the eclipse begins to clear immediately as the shadow continues to move.

تقاطع القطرين
فضل نصف القطر
انخساف جزئي
انخساف كلي مع مكث
لا مكث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any total lunar eclipse must have a period of duration (makkth).
Correction: A total eclipse can be instantaneous (no duration) if the moon only grazes the inner edge of the shadow, as when the latitude exactly equals the excess of the shadow's radius over the moon's radius.

Checkpoint
What happens when the moon's latitude is less than the excess of the shadow's radius over the moon's radius?
A) The moon is not eclipsed at all.
B) The moon is partially eclipsed.
C) The moon is totally eclipsed with duration.
D) The moon is totally eclipsed but without duration.

Key Takeaway
The type and duration of a lunar eclipse depend on the moon's latitude relative to the shadow's radius and the moon's radius.

The precision of these conditions—a single degree of latitude changes everything—reflects the exactness of Allah's creation. Solar Eclipse: Cause and Distinction from Lunar
Explains cause of solar eclipse and distinction from lunar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astronomical cause of a solar eclipse and how it differs from a lunar eclipse in terms of physical change and observation.

Core Idea
A solar eclipse is a visual obscuration, not a change in the sun — unlike the moon which truly darkens.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explains the natural cause of a solar eclipse: the moon passes between the earth and the sun, blocking its light. It also clarifies the key difference from a lunar eclipse: a solar eclipse involves no change in the sun itself, only a covering, while a lunar eclipse is an actual darkening of the moon. Understanding this distinction helps prevent theological misunderstandings about celestial events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's description of the cause and distinction settle before interpreting.
The passage includes both the original text and the commentator's clarification.
2
Identify the cause of solar eclipse
Find the condition that must be met for a solar eclipse to occur. Notice the terms 'visible conjunction', 'no visible latitude', and 'line of sight'.
3
Understand the distinction from lunar eclipse
The passage explicitly contrasts solar and lunar eclipses. Identify what differs: in a solar eclipse, the sun itself does not change; in a lunar eclipse, the moon does change.
This is the core doctrinal point.
4
Reflect on the angular size explanation
The passage explains why the moon can completely cover the sun despite being smaller. Think about apparent size and distance.
This is a natural phenomenon, not a miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : كسوف الشمس
في كسوف الشمس) فنقول ( عند اجتماع القمر بالشمس في النهار اجتماعاً مرئياً لا حقيقياً ( إن لم يكن للقمر عرض ( مرئي ( حجب بيننا وبين الشمس) ) لوقوعه على الخط الخارج من أبصارنا إليها ( فلم نر ضوء الشمس بل نرى لون القمر الكمد في وجه الشمس فنظن أن الشمس ذهب ضوؤها وهو الكسوف) فليس الكسوف بغير حال في ذات الشمس كالخسوف في ذات القمر ولذلك أمكن أن يقع كسوف بالقياس إلى قوم دون قوم ) ويكون ذلك بقدر صفحة القمر فربما كسف الشمس كلها وإن كان أصغر منها ) وذلك ) لأنه أقرب إلينا في وتر قطره الزاوية التي توترها الشمس (كمداً فتحجب به عنا بتمامها. وربما تكون الشمس) وقت
قوله : ( اجتماعاً مرئياً لا حقيقاً) وذلك لأن الشمس والقمر ليسا في فلك واحد حتى يجتمعان اجتماعاً حقيقياً . وقوله : يوترها الشمس أي يوترها قطر الشمس .
The Third Objective: Solar Eclipse
Concerning solar eclipse: we say that when the moon conjuncts the sun in the daytime—a visible conjunction, not a real one—if the moon has no visible latitude, it veils between us and the sun, because it lies on the line extending from our eyes to the sun. So we do not see the sun's light, but we see the dim color of the moon in the face of the sun, and we think that the sun's light has gone—that is the eclipse. So the eclipse is not a change in the sun itself, like the lunar eclipse is a change in the moon itself. Therefore, it is possible that an eclipse occurs relative to some people and not to others. And that [the amount eclipsed] is according to the size of the moon's disk, so sometimes it may eclipse the entire sun even though it is smaller, because it is nearer to us, and the angle subtended by its diameter subtends the same angle as the sun's diameter, so it veils it completely from us. And sometimes the sun at the time...
His saying: 'a visible conjunction, not a real one': that is because the sun and the moon are not in one sphere such that they have a real conjunction. And his saying: 'subtends the sun': i.e., its diameter subtends the angle of the sun's diameter.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines solar eclipse and distinguishes it from lunar eclipse. It begins by stating that a solar eclipse occurs when the moon is in visible conjunction with the sun during the day—meaning it passes between the earth and the sun—and if the moon has no visible latitude (i.e., it is exactly on the line of sight), it blocks the sun from our view. We then see the moon's dim color over the sun's face, mistakenly thinking the sun has lost its light; in reality, the eclipse is merely an obscuration. Unlike a lunar eclipse, which is an actual darkening of the moon's body, a solar eclipse does not involve any change in the sun itself; it is a local phenomenon—only some observers see it. The amount of the sun covered depends on the apparent size of the moon's disk; because the moon is closer to us, its angular size can equal or exceed that of the sun, allowing it to cover the entire sun despite being smaller in absolute size. The commentary clarifies that the conjunction is only apparent, not real, because the sun and moon are not in the same orbital plane. It also explains that 'subtends' refers to the angular diameter.

Key Terms
كسوف الشمس
اجتماع مرئي
عرض مرئي
حجب
صفحة القمر
وتر الزاوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that during a solar eclipse the sun itself darkens or changes.
Correction: The passage clearly states that the eclipse is not a change in the sun itself; it is only an obscuration by the moon.

Checkpoint
Why can the moon sometimes completely cover the sun even though it is smaller?
A) Because the sun is dimmer.
B) Because the moon is closer to us and has a similar angular size.
C) Because the moon expands temporarily.
D) Because the sun moves behind the moon.

Key Takeaway
Solar eclipses are local obscurations, not changes in the sun, while lunar eclipses are actual darkenings of the moon.

?
This lesson shows that what you see is not always what is real—the sun does not actually darken. Name one person you have judged unfairly and commit to seeing them as they truly are before Allah.

Al-Amidi's Method: Exposing Baseless Superstitions
Al-Amidi states his purpose: exposing baseless opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's purpose in recounting the opinions of naturalists: not to endorse them, but to demonstrate their lack of rational foundation and to affirm the kalam methodology of refutation.

Core Idea
Al-Amidi reports opinions to show they have no proof.

In the previous passages, al-Amidi presented various opinions about celestial phenomena from natural philosophers. Now he clarifies his intention: he is not relaying these views as viable alternatives, but rather to expose their weakness. This lesson unpacks his sharp critique and the methodological principle behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Amidi's statement of purpose
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp al-Amidi's explicit goal: to reveal the baselessness of these opinions.
Notice the strong language: 'superstitions,' 'corrupt fantasies,' 'cold deceptions.'
2
Identify the key evaluative terms
Look for words like 'خرافات', 'لا ثبت', 'خيالات فاسدة', 'تمويهات باردة'. These show al-Amidi's dismissive stance.
3
Understand the kalam methodology
Reflect on why al-Amidi chooses to list and then refute. This is a common kalam strategy: after presenting opposing views, the author demolishes them to strengthen the orthodox position.
This passage is a methodological marker.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to verify you grasp al-Amidi's purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الآمدي : والغرض من نقل هذه الاختلافات إبداء ما ذكروه من الخرافات ليتحقق ويتبين للعاقل الفطن أنه لا ثبت أي لا حجة لهم فيما يقولونه ويعتقدونه ولا معول على ما ينقلونه من أوائلهم ويعتمدونه، وإنما هي خيالات فاسدة وتمويهات باردة يظهر ضعفها بأوائل النظر ثم البعض بالبعض يعتبر.
Al-Amidi said: And the purpose of reporting these differences is to expose what they mentioned of superstitions, so that it may be established and become clear to the intelligent, perceptive person that there is no proof—that is, no argument for them in what they say and believe, and no reliance on what they transmit from their predecessors and adopt. Rather, they are corrupt fantasies and cold deceptions, whose weakness appears at the beginning of reflection, and then some of them are considered by others.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents al-Amidi's explicit purpose for recounting these opinions. He states that he is not endorsing any of them but rather exposing them as baseless superstitions. The goal is to demonstrate that none of these naturalistic explanations have any valid evidence or rational foundation. They are 'corrupt fantasies' and 'cold deceptions' that reveal their weakness upon minimal scrutiny. This reflects the kalam methodology of refuting alternative views to strengthen the case for divine creation and the limitations of human reason in explaining celestial phenomena.

What prevents you from applying this first-glance critical reflection to what you currently believe?
?

The Refutation of All Alternatives Requirement
First two objections against four-element doctrine: refutation impossibility and non-entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two methodological objections to proving the four-element doctrine: the impossibility of refuting all alternatives and the non-entailment of four even if alternatives are false.

Core Idea
Proving a doctrine requires refuting all alternatives, and even that may not prove it.

In this passage, the author presents two objections against the claim that the elements are four. The first objection targets the impossibility of complete refutation; the second shows that even complete refutation does not logically entail a specific alternative. These objections highlight the methodological rigor required in theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the two objections.
Notice how each objection begins with a conditional structure.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection argues that complete refutation of all alternatives is required but impossible. The second grants refutation but questions the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the method
Consider why these objections are presented and what they imply about proving doctrinal claims.
This is a key methodological insight in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكفي في إثبات كون العناصر أربعة إبطال بعضها أي بعض هذه الأقوال الخمسة المنافية له بالحجة، بل لا بد في إثباته من إبطال الجميع وهو مما لا سبيل إليه سلمنا بطلان هذه الأقوال بأسرها لكن ليس يلزم ذلك كونها أربعة إذ لقائل أن يقول : لم قلتم : إن الأجسام ليست متجانسة فيكون الاختلاف حينئذ فيما بينها لا في الصور المقومة والطبائع الجوهرية بل في الصفات للفاعل المختار سلمنا أنها أربعة لكن لا نسلم ما ذكر من أحوالها بل نقول : فلم لا يجوز أن تكون كلها خفيفة طالبة للمحيط أو تكون كلها ثقيلة طالبة للمركز ويكون ما فيها من التفاوت في الأحياز لتفاوتها في الثقل والخفة فالأثقل أسبق إلى المركز من الثقيل الطالب له أيضاً والأخف أسبق إلى المحيط من الخفيف الذي يطلبه ألا ترى أن الأجسام الأرضية المتشاركة في أصل الثقل تتفاوت قوله : (فلم لا يجوز أن تكون اختلافها في الميل الصاعد والهابط يدل على أن كلها ليست خفيفة ولا ثقيلة أي يسجد به ظاهر سطحه على سطحه بنسبة واحدة. وذلك لأنها لو كانت فيما بينها لكان الطلوع على الجميع والغروب عنه دفعة ولو كانت مقعرة لانعكس الأمر في الارتفاع والانحطاط
And it is not sufficient in proving that the elements are four to refute some of them, i.e., some of these five sayings opposed to it by argument, rather in proving it it is necessary to refute all of them, and that is impossible. Granted the falsity of all these sayings, still that does not entail that they are four, for one may say: why do you say that bodies are not homogeneous? Then the difference [between them] would be in their accidents, not in the constitutive forms and essential natures, but in the attributes [given] by the free agent. Granted that they are four, still we do not grant what you mentioned of their states, but we say: why is it not permissible that all of them be light, seeking the periphery, or all be heavy, seeking the center, and that the difference in their places be due to their difference in heaviness and lightness? For the heavier is more prior to the center than the heavy that also seeks it, and the lighter is more prior to the periphery than the light that seeks it. Do you not see that earthly bodies sharing in the root of heaviness differ? [Margin note]: His saying: 'why is it not permissible that their difference in ascending and descending inclination indicates that all are neither light nor heavy?' i.e., [the critique] that the apparent surface of one surfaces on another with a single proportion, because if they were [concave] among themselves, then rising and setting would be for all at once, and if they were concave, the matter would be reversed in elevation and depression.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents three objections against the four-element doctrine. First, proving the four elements requires refuting all alternative theories, not just some, and that is impossible. Second, even if all alternatives are false, it does not prove four; bodies could differ by accident given a free agent. Third, even if there are four, they could all be light or all heavy, with differences in place due to degrees of lightness/heaviness. The margin note addresses a response: the difference in ascending and descending inclination shows they are not all light or all heavy, because if they were all light, celestial spheres would have uniform motion, etc.

Key Terms
إبطال
بطلان
الصور المقومة
الطبائع الجوهرية
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disproving a few alternatives is sufficient to prove a positive claim.
Correction: In rigorous argument, one must refute all alternatives, which is often impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the first objection claim is required to prove the four elements?
A) Refute some alternatives
B) Refute all alternatives
C) Provide direct evidence
D) Rely on authority

Key Takeaway
Proving a positive claim like the four elements requires refuting all alternatives, which is often impractical; even then, the conclusion may not follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The phrase 'attributes of the free agent' points to God's direct action. Do you truly see the diversity and differences in creation as directly from God's will, or do you default to naturalistic explanations that distance Him? How does this lesson press you to recognize God's hand in everything, even the differences you might attribute to chance or nature?

The Objection of Degrees and the Response from Inclination
Third objection: all elements could be light or heavy; response: inclination disproves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that all four elements could be light or heavy with differences in degree, and the response that their inclination in motion disproves this.

Core Idea
Even if there are four elements, their properties could be explained by degrees of lightness or heaviness, but their motion disproves it.

This lesson presents the third objection against the four-element doctrine: that all elements could be fundamentally light or heavy, with differences in place explained by degrees. Then the margin note provides an answer based on ascending and descending motion, showing that not all are light or all heavy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection

2
Read the response

Consider the reasoning about celestial bodies.
3
Examine the reasoning
The response uses the behavior of rising and setting to show that if all were light or all heavy, motion would be uniform.
This is a key physical argument.

Definition of the Equator and Equality of Day and Night
Defines the equator and explains equality of day and night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the Earth's equator and why day and night are always equal there.

Core Idea
At the equator, the horizon cuts the celestial sphere into equal halves, making day and night always equal.

This passage from the kalam tradition defines the equator and explains a key astronomical phenomenon. It shows how the Earth's geometry and the horizon's relationship lead to equal day and night at the equator. The author also notes a minor, imperceptible variation due to the Sun's orbit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's wording establish the concepts.
Notice the technical terms like 'خط الاستواء' and 'معدل النهار'.
2
Focus on the definition of the equator
Identify how the equator is defined in relation to the celestial equator and the horizon. Note that the horizon passes through the celestial poles.
3
Understand the reason for equal day and night
The passage explains that day and night are equal because the horizon cuts the daily orbits into equal halves. Consider why this is unique to the equator.
The exception due to apogee/perigee is mentioned but deemed negligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السابع : المدارات اليومية للأرض ] ما يوازى من الأرض معدل النهار أي الدائرة العظيمة على سطح الأرض في سطح معدل النهار الموازية لمحيطه يسمى خط الاستواء والأفق يقطع المعدل وجميع المدارات اليومية فيه بنصفين على قوائم لمروره بقطبي المعدل وتلك المدارات فيكون الليل والنهار هناك في جميع السنة سواء لتساوي قوسيهما الواقعة إحداهما تحت الأفق والأخرى فوقه فلا يقع بينهما تفاوت إلا باختلاف حركة الشمس في السرعة والبطء بواسطة الأوج والحضيض وذلك مما لا يحس به ولا يلتفت إليه ، وأما في غير ذلك الموضع الذي هو تحت المعدل فيقطع الأفق
The seventh section: The daily orbits of the Earth. That which is parallel from the Earth to the celestial equator, i.e., the great circle on the Earth's surface in the plane of the celestial equator parallel to its circumference, is called the equator. And the horizon cuts the celestial equator and all the daily orbits in it into two halves at right angles because it passes through the poles of the celestial equator and those orbits. So night and day there (at the equator) are equal throughout the entire year, because the two arcs of them (night and day) are equal, one of them being below the horizon and the other above it. So no difference occurs between them except due to the variation of the Sun's motion in speed and slowness by means of apogee and perigee, and that is not perceived nor taken into consideration. As for in other than that location which is under the celestial equator, the horizon cuts
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines the Earth's equator and explains its astronomical significance. It begins by stating that the line on Earth parallel to the celestial equator, called the equator, is a great circle on the Earth's surface lying in the same plane as the celestial equator. The horizon, at the equator, cuts the celestial equator and all daily orbits (the paths of stars) into two equal halves at right angles because the horizon passes through the celestial poles. As a result, day and night are always equal in length at the equator throughout the year, since the arcs of daylight and darkness are equal — one is below the horizon and the other above. The only minor variation comes from the Sun's non-uniform motion due to its apogee and perigee, but this is imperceptible and ignored. The passage then begins to discuss locations away from the equator, but is cut off here.

Proof of the Four Elements in Compounds by Analysis
Proves the four elements exist in compounds through distillation and volume argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of analysis (distillation) was used to demonstrate that composite bodies contain the elements earth, water, and air, with fire implied as the agent.

Core Idea
Distillation reveals that compounds contain hidden elements.

This passage presents a key argument from the classical tradition for the existence of the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) in composite substances. Using the method of analysis (al-tahlil), the author describes a distillation experiment that separates a compound into its constituent parts. By comparing volumes before and after separation, the presence of air is inferred. This lesson walks through the logical steps of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the method: analysis vs synthesis
The passage introduces two methods: analysis (breaking down) and synthesis (building up). Here, only analysis is discussed.
Synthesis is mentioned as another approach but not elaborated here.
2
Follow the distillation process
A compound is placed in a flask (qar‘) and alembic (inbiq). Heating separates aqueous and earthy parts.
The alembic is the distillation apparatus.
3
Observe the volume discrepancy
After separation, the volume of the collected earth and water is less than the original compound's volume.
This is the key observation.
4
Infer the presence of air
The volume gap shows that something else was present in the compound to fill the space. That something is air, which causes rarefaction.
Without air, the compound would be solid and dense, and the separated parts would fill the same volume.
5
Understand the conclusion
Thus the compound contains at least three elements: earth, water, and air. Fire is implied as the separating agent and also as a constituent.
The passage ends by affirming these are the 'pillars' (arkan) present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثاني عشر : العناصر الأربعة والمركبات ] زعموا أن هذه العناصر الأربعة هي الأركان التي تتركب منها المركبات ويثبتونه بطريق التحليل تارةً والتركيب أخرى. فالأول: أنا إذا جعلنا مركباً في القرع والإنبيق انفصل عنه أجزاء مائية و أجزاء أرضية فدل ذلك على أن هذين العنصرين كانا موجودين فيه مختلطين ففرقتهما الحرارة ولا شك أن ثمة أي في ذلك المركب أجزاء هوائية بها تخلخل الأجزاء الأرضية والمائية التي فيه وإلا لكان ذلك المركب في غاية الاندماج والرصانة ولكان ما يحصل بالتفريق من العنصرين حجمه إذا ضم بعضه إِلى بعض كالذي كان للمركب عند التركيب، فيثبت وجود الهواء فيه ولا شك أنها أي الأركان المذكورة الموجودة في المركب.
The Twelfth Intention: The Four Elements and Compounds. They claimed that these four elements are the pillars from which compounds are composed, and they prove it sometimes by the method of analysis and sometimes by synthesis. The first: If we place a compound in a flask and alembic, separate from it aqueous parts and earthy parts. That indicates that these two elements were present in it mixed, and heat separated them. There is no doubt that in that compound there are aerial parts by which the earthy and aqueous parts in it become rarefied. Otherwise, that compound would be in extreme compaction and solidity, and the volume obtained by separation of the two elements, if some of it is joined to the other, would equal what the compound had at the time of composition. Thus the existence of air in it is established. There is no doubt that these are the mentioned pillars present in the compound.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) as constituents of composite bodies. The author presents the method of analysis (al-tahlil), specifically distillation using a flask and alembic. When a compound is heated, it separates into aqueous and earthy parts, showing that water and earth were present. But the volume of the separated parts is less than the original compound, indicating that something else filled the space. That something is air, which causes rarefaction of the solid and liquid parts. If air were not present, the compound would be very dense and solid, and the volume of the separated earth and water would equal the original volume. Since the separated volume is smaller, air must have been present. Thus, the compound contains water, earth, and air. The passage does not explicitly mention fire, but presumably heat is the agent and maybe the fourth element is implied. The author says this is the first proof (by analysis), implying a second proof by synthesis (not given here). The passage ends by affirming that these three elements (and by implication the fourth, fire) are present in the compound as its 'pillars' (arkan).

Refuting al-Katibi's Eightfold Deviation Claim
The author refutes al-Katibi's claim that deviation from medical moderation is limited to eight types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Katibi's claim that deviation from moderate temperament is limited to eight types is flawed, and how the author demonstrates the need to consider both quantity and quality.

Core Idea
Deviation from moderation is not just about quality; quantity matters too.

Al-Katibi argued that deviation from moderate temperament is limited to eight types. But the author shows that this claim overlooks the role of quantity. By examining the meaning of 'appropriate portion' from elements, the author reveals that true moderation involves both quantity and quality, leading to more possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-Katibi's claim
Al-Katibi claimed that deviation from moderate temperament is limited to eight types. This is the claim being refuted.
This claim is based on considering only qualities.
2
Grasp the meaning of 'his portion'
The author explains that 'his portion that is appropriate for him from the elements' means observing proportion in both quantities (number) and qualities (strength/weakness).
3
See the refutation
The author states that al-Katibi's assumption becomes invalid because deviation can occur in quantity alone, quality alone, or both.
This undermines the limit of eight.
4
Discuss true moderation
The author points out that true moderation considers equality in both quantity and quality, so deviation in quality alone yields eight types, but there are additional types from quantity or both.
Only if moderation is restricted to quality does the opposition become eight.
5
Reflect on the exception
If true moderation is defined by quality only, then al-Katibi's claim holds. But the author suggests this is insufficient.
The full definition includes quantity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه قسطه الذي ينبغي له من العناصر بكمياتها وكيفياتها معناه رعاية النسبة بين كمياتها في العدد وكيفياتها في القوة والضعف وحينئذ بطل ما توهمه الكاتبي من أن الخارج عن المعتدل بحسب الطب لا ينحصر في ثمانية. ثم إنه ادعى أن الخروج إذ قيس إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي انحصر أقسامه في الثمانية، وفيه أيضاً بحث لأن الحقيقي اعتبر فيه تساوي الكميات والكيفيات معا على ما عرفت، فالخارج عنه في الكيفية وحدها ثمانية وتبقى هناك أقسام أخر بحسب الكمية وحدها أو بحسبهما معاً . نعم إذا اكتفى في المعتدل الحقيقي باعتبار التساوي في الكيفيات فقط انحصر ما يقابله في ثمانية أيضا .
His portion that is appropriate for him from the elements in their quantities and qualities means observing the proportion between their quantities in number and their qualities in strength and weakness, and then what al-Katibi imagined becomes invalid, that what deviates from the moderate according to medicine is not limited to eight. Then he claimed that deviation when measured against true moderation is limited to eight types, but there is also discussion in this because in the true [moderate] equality of quantities and qualities together is considered as you know, so deviation from it in quality alone is eight, and there remain other divisions according to quantity alone or both together. Yes, if in true moderation one suffices with consideration of equality in qualities only, then what opposes it is also limited to eight.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim by al-Katibi. The author begins by explaining the meaning of 'his portion that is appropriate for him from the elements' as maintaining proportion in both quantities (number) and qualities (strength/weakness). Then he states that al-Katibi's assumption that deviation from the medical moderate temperament is not limited to eight types is invalid. Al-Katibi later argued that when measured against true moderation, the types of deviation are limited to eight. The author finds this problematic because true moderation requires equality in both quantity and quality simultaneously. Deviations in quality alone yield eight types, but there are additional types based on quantity or a combination of both. Only if we restrict true moderation to equality in quality alone does the opposition become limited to eight. Thus, the passage carefully exposes the oversight in al-Katibi's reasoning.

Why Perfection is Better than Form
Arguing that defining soul as perfection is superior to defining it as form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and linguistic reasons why defining the soul as 'perfection' is considered superior to defining it as 'form'.

Core Idea
Perfection indicates the species and implies matter; form indicates only matter.

In this passage, the commentator presents a refined argument for preferring 'perfection' (kamal) over 'form' (sura) in the definition of the soul. The reasoning involves the relation of these terms to the species (naw') and matter (madda), as well as the nature of linguistic indication. Let us follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice the technical terms: كمال, نوع, جنس, مادة, صورة.
The passage is dense; reading twice is helpful.
2
Identify the two main reasons
The argument gives two reasons: (1) kamal is related to the species, which is closer to the genus, unlike sura which relates to matter; (2) the species includes matter, so kamal implicitly indicates matter, whereas sura does not indicate the species.
The commentator elaborates on linguistic usage of 'form'.
3
Understand the commentary
The commentary explains that 'form' usually means bodily form, not specific form, hence no indication of the species. This strengthens the case for kamal.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this argument matters for defining the soul. The definition should be as complete and unambiguous as possible.
This is a methodological point in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه ( أي الكمال ( مقيس إلى النوع وهو ( أي النوع ( أقرب إلى طبيعة الجنس ) لصحة الحمل بينهما ) من المادة التي تقاس إليها الصورة ) إذ لا حمل بينهما ولا شك أن وضع المنسوب إلى ما هو أقرب إلى الجنس مكانه أولى من وضع المنسوب إلى ما ليس أقرب ( كيف ؟ ) أي كيف لا يكون تعريفها بالكمال أولى ( والمادة يتضمنها النوع من غير عكس ؟ فإذا دل بالكمال على النوع فقد دل ضمناً على المادة بخلاف ما إذا دل بالصورة على المادة، إذ لا دلالة حينئذ على النوع فالدلالة الأولى أكمل من الثانية. قوله : ( ولا شك أن وضع المنسوب) أي وضع الكمال مثلاً المنسوب إلى ما هو أقرب أي إلى النوع الذي هو أقرب إلى الجنس مكانه أي مكان الجنس أولى من وضع المنسوب أي من وضع الصورة مثلا المنسوبة إلى ما ليس أقرب أي إلى المادة التي ليست أقرب إلى الجنس كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( إذ لا دلالة حينئذ على النوع وذلك لأنه المتبادر من إطلاق الصورة هو الصورة الجسمية على ما هو الكثير الشائع أو مطلق الصورة المتناول للصورتين أي النوعية والجسمية وأياً ما كان فلا دلالة حينئذ للنوع، ولا يتبادر الصورة النوعية من إطلاق اللفظ حتى يتصور هناك دلالة على النوع.
And because it (i.e., perfection) is measured against the species, and it (i.e., the species) is closer to the nature of the genus (due to the validity of predication between them) than the matter against which the form is measured (since there is no predication between them). And there is no doubt that placing that which is relative to what is closer to the genus in its place is more appropriate than placing that which is relative to what is not closer. (How?) That is, how is defining it by perfection not more appropriate? And the species includes matter without the converse. So if by perfection one indicates the species, one has implicitly indicated matter, unlike when one indicates matter by form, for then there is no indication of the species. Therefore the first indication is more complete than the second. His saying: (And there is no doubt that placing the relative) meaning placing perfection, for example, relative to what is closer, i.e., the species which is closer to the genus, in its place, i.e., the place of the genus, is more appropriate than placing the relative, i.e., form, for example, relative to what is not closer, i.e., matter which is not closer to the genus, as is not hidden. His saying: (For then there is no indication of the species) and that is because what is immediately understood from the unrestricted use of 'form' is the bodily form, according to what is common and frequent, or the absolute form encompassing both forms, i.e., the specific form and the bodily form; in either case there is no indication of the species, and the specific form is not immediately understood from the unrestricted use of the term so that one could imagine an indication of the species.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for preferring to define the soul (nafs) as 'perfection' (kamal) rather than as 'form' (sura). The argument proceeds in two stages. First, the author explains that kamal is measured against the species (naw'), which is closer to the genus (jins) than matter (madda) is. Since the relationship between kamal and species is closer, placing kamal in the definition is better than placing sura. Second, the author notes that the species includes matter without the converse; thus, defining with kamal indirectly indicates matter, whereas defining with sura does not indicate the species. The commentator clarifies that when sura is mentioned, it typically refers to the bodily form (sura jismiyya) or absolute form, not specifically the specific form (sura naw'iyya), so it does not indicate the species. Therefore, the definition using kamal is more complete.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows that 'perfection' is more comprehensive because it indicates the species and implicitly matter, while 'form' primarily indicates matter without necessarily indicating the species. Therefore, 'perfection' is a better choice.

Why Defining the Soul as Perfection Is Better Than Power
Two reasons for preferring perfection over power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'perfection' (kamal) is preferred over 'power' (quwwa) for defining the soul in Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
Perfection encompasses both active and passive powers, eliminating the ambiguity of 'power'.

In defining the soul, philosophers debated whether to call it a 'perfection' or a 'power'. The author argues that 'perfection' is superior because 'power' is ambiguous and incomplete. This lesson unpacks the two reasons he gives for this preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Note the two reasons presented.
The passage uses conjunctions like 'ولأن' to introduce reasons.
2
Understand the ambiguity of 'quwwa'
Identify how 'quwwa' can mean active or passive power, and why this ruins a definition.
The term is homonymous (ishtirak lafzi).
3
Compare with 'kamal'
See how 'kamal' covers both aspects without ambiguity and defines the soul from all aspects.
This is the second reason: completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا تعريف النفس بالكمال أولى من القوة لأنها للانفعال وللقوة الفعل ليست بمعنى واحد ) يعنى أن لفظة القوة تطلق بالاشتراك اللفظي على معنيين قوة الفعل وقوة الانفعال، وللنفس قوة الإدراك وهي انفعالية وقوة التحريك وهي فعلية وليس اعتبار إحداهما أولى من اعتبار الأخرى ولا يجوز اعتبارهما معا فيفسد الحد بخلاف لفظ الكمال فإنه يتناولهما بمعنى واحد فلا محذور فيه . ( ولأن القوة اسم لها) أي للنفس (من حيث هي مبدأ الآثار وهو بعض جهاته) أي جهات هذا المعرف فتعرفه من هذه الجهة فقط ، ( والكمال اسم لها من حيث يتم بها الحقيقة) النوعية المستتبعة لآثارها ( فتعرفها من جميع جهاته. ولا ريب في أن تعريف الشيء بجميع جهاته أولى من تعريفه ببعضها . قوله : وللنفس قوة الإدراك وهو مثل القوة النظرية للنفس الإنسانية. وقوله : فتعرفه أي فتعرف القوة. وقوله : اسم لها أي للنفس وقوله فيعرفه أي يعرف الكمال هذا المعرف.
And likewise, defining the soul by perfection is more appropriate than by power, because power is for passivity and active power are not one meaning. That is, the word 'power' (quwwa) is used by homonymy for two meanings: active power and passive power. The soul has a power of perception, which is passive, and a power of motion, which is active. Considering one of them is not more appropriate than considering the other, and it is not permissible to consider them together, for that would ruin the definition. Unlike the word 'perfection' (kamal), for it encompasses both with a single meaning, so there is no problem in it. And because 'power' is a name for it (i.e., the soul) insofar as it is a principle of effects, which is one of its aspects, so it defines it from this aspect only. And 'perfection' is a name for it insofar as through it the specific reality comprising its effects is completed, so it defines it from all its aspects. There is no doubt that defining a thing from all its aspects is more appropriate than defining it from some of them. His saying: And for the soul there is a power of perception, and it is like the theoretical power of the human soul. And his saying: So it defines it, meaning it defines power. And his saying: a name for it, meaning for the soul. And his saying: so it defines it, meaning it defines perfection, this definer.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents two reasons for preferring to define the soul as perfection (kamal) rather than as power (quwwa). First, the term 'quwwa' is ambiguous (ishtirak lafzi) between active power (quwwa al-fi'l) and passive power (quwwa al-infi'al). The soul has both: a power of perception (passive) and a power of motion (active). Choosing one would be arbitrary, and taking both together would ruin the definition, whereas kamal covers both meanings unambiguously. Second, 'quwwa' names the soul only as a principle of effects, one aspect, while 'kamal' names it as that through which the specific reality (al-haqiqa al-naw'iyya) is completed, encompassing all aspects. The commentator adds that the power of perception is like the theoretical power of the human soul, and clarifies the referents of pronouns in the original text.

Key Terms
كمال
قوة
اشتراك لفظي
قوة الفعل
قوة الانفعال
مبدأ الآثار
حقيقة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'power' is a clear term, but its ambiguity undermines the definition.
Correction: The word 'power' (quwwa) is used homonymously for active and passive powers, leading to ambiguity in defining the soul.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that defining the soul as 'perfection' (kamal) is better than defining it as 'power' (quwwa)?
A) Because 'power' is a synonym for 'perfection'
B) Because 'power' is ambiguous between active and passive meanings, while 'perfection' covers both
C) Because 'power' is only active, missing the passive aspect
D) Because 'perfection' is a more modern term

Key Takeaway
Defining the soul as 'perfection' avoids the ambiguity of 'power' and captures its full reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own soul, do you tend to focus on what you can do (active power) or what you receive (passive power), or do you see yourself as a complete reality (kamal) that encompasses both? In what ways might this limited perspective affect your relationship with Allah?
?
This lesson warns against using ambiguous terms to define the soul. Are there areas in your faith where you use terms loosely without a single, clear meaning—for example, 'faith' itself, 'love of Allah', or 'taqwa'? How could you seek a more precise understanding that captures the whole reality?
?
If you were to define your own purpose in life using either 'power' (what you can achieve or endure) or 'perfection' (the complete reality Allah intended for you), which would you choose? What practical step can you take today to align your self-definition with the comprehensive view this lesson advocates?

Including the Object of Relation in Definitions
Explaining how to define things by their relations including extrinsic elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, when defining things by their relational names, it is necessary to include the object of relation (al-mudaf ilayh) even if it is not part of the thing's substance.

Core Idea
Relational names require extrinsic elements in definitions.

This passage from al-Sanusi explains a key methodological principle in defining things by their relations. When we define something not by its essence but by its relation to something else—such as 'father' or 'head'—we must include the object of that relation. This may seem counterintuitive since the object is external, but it becomes essential for that specific naming.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the two main parts: the principle statement and the clarification with examples.
Pay attention to the term 'mudaf ilayh' (object of relation).
2
Identify the principle and its justification
The central claim is that when defining from the 'second perspective' (relation), we must include the object of relation. The justification is that this object becomes dhati (essential) from the perspective of the name (min jihat al-tasmiya).
Contrast this with definitions based on essence (dhat), where only intrinsic properties are included.
3
Apply the principle to the given examples
The passage gives two types of things: those with both an essential and a relational name (e.g., agent, father) and those with only a relational name (e.g., head, hand). Reflect on how the definition of 'father' must include 'child', and the definition of 'head' must include 'body'.
Consider how this principle affects the definition of relational concepts in general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آخر فإذا أردنا تعريفه من الجهة الثانية فلا بد أن يأخذ فيه المضاف إليه وهي ) أي الأمور المضاف إليها ( وإن لم تكن ذاتية (لها أي للأشياء التي أريد تعريفها (في جوهرها فهي (ذاتية) لها ( من جهة التسمية). وتوضيحه ما في المباحث المشرقية أن الشيء قد يكون له في ذاته، وجوهره اسم يخصه وباعتبار إضافته إلى غيره اسم آخر كالفاعل والمنفعل والأب والابن وقد لا يكون له اسم إلا باعتبار إضافته إلى غيره كالرأس واليد والجناح . فمتى أردنا أن نعطيها حدودها من جهة أسمائها بما هي مضافة أخذنا الأشياء الخارجة عن جواهرها في حدودها لأنها ذاتيات لها بحسب الأسماء التي لها تلك الحدود
Another: So if we want to define it from the second perspective, then it is necessary to include in it the object of relation (al-mudaf ilayh), i.e., the things to which it is related, even if they are not essential (dhatiyya) for it (i.e., for the things we want to define) in their substance, yet they are essential for them from the perspective of naming. Its clarification is what is in the 'Mabahith Mashriqiyya': that a thing may have in its essence and substance a name specific to it, and by virtue of its addition to something else another name, like agent and patient, father and son. And it may have no name except by virtue of its addition to something else, like head, hand, and wing. So whenever we want to give them their definitions from the perspective of their names insofar as they are relatives, we take things external to their substances in their definitions, because they are essential for them according to the names for which those definitions are given.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage explains the methodological principle for defining things by their relations (al-idaafa). When we want to define something from the perspective of its relation to another (al-jiha al-thaniya), we must include the object of relation (al-mudaf ilayh) in the definition, even if that object is not essential (dhati) to the thing's substance (jawhar). It is essential, however, from the perspective of the name (min jihat al-tasmiya). The author illustrates with examples from the 'Mabahith Mashriqiyya': a thing may have one name by its essence and another by its relation (e.g., agent, patient, father, son), or may have no name except by relation (e.g., head, hand, wing). When giving definitions based on such relational names, we include extrinsic elements (al-ashya' al-kharija 'an jawahiriha) because they are essential for the name.

Key Terms
إضافة
مضاف إليه
ذاتي
جوهر
تسمية
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A definition should never include elements that are not part of the thing's essence or substance.
Correction: This passage shows that when defining by a relational name, we must include the object of relation, even if it is extrinsic to the substance, because it is essential for that specific name.

Checkpoint
Why must the object of relation (al-mudaf ilayh) be included in the definition when defining a thing from the perspective of its relation?
A) Because it is always essential to the thing's substance
B) Because it is essential for the name under which it is being defined
C) Because it is a necessary part of the form
D) Because the definition would otherwise be incomplete

Key Takeaway
When defining by a relational name, the object of that relation must be included in the definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own identity, do you tend to define yourself by what you are in yourself (e.g., personality traits) or by your relationships (e.g., as a child, parent, friend)? According to this lesson, the relational name requires including the 'other' in the definition. Are you willing to let others define part of who you are? Or do you resist that dependence?
?
Think of a role you have that only exists because of someone else — like 'student' (needs a teacher) or 'leader' (needs followers). Have you ever tried to define that role as if it were independent? This lesson says the object of relation must be included. How does it change the way you understand that role—and your responsibility toward the other person?
?
What if there is something in your life that you only recognize by its relation to you — like 'my health' or 'my faith'? Have you ever tried to define that thing without mentioning yourself? This lesson warns that excludes what is essential for the name. What would it mean for you to include yourself (or your relationship) in the definition of something you deeply value, right now?

Refuting the Strength-Based Argument for Multiple Faculties
Sanusi refutes a proof for multiple faculties based on strength/weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the observation of strength and weakness in organs does not necessarily prove distinct faculties.

Core Idea
Strength differences in organs may be due to instruments, not distinct faculties.

Before we can evaluate the doctrine of multiple faculties, we must examine the key argument used to support it: the observation that an organ can be strong in one function and weak in another. The author shows this argument is logically weak, because the difference could come from the instrument itself, not from a difference in the essence of the faculty. This lesson analyzes that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and English translation to get the gist. Notice how the author presents the opposing view before criticizing it.
2
Identify the opposing argument
The passage begins with 'ما يقال' – what is said. Identify the claim: that strength in one faculty and weakness in another proves distinct faculties.
The author labels this argument as 'weak' (ضعيف).
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues the difference could be due to a weak instrument (الآلة), not a weak faculty. Grasp why this undermines the proof for multiplicity.
The key phrase is 'لضعف الآلة واختلاف فيها'.
4
Ask: Why is this significant?
Consider how this refutation fits into the larger debate about whether faculties are multiple or one. It shows that empirical observations need careful interpretation.
This connects to the broader critique of philosophical psychology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وما يقال ) في بيان تعدد القوى ( أنا نرى العضو قويا في إحداها ) أي إحدى القوى (وضعيفاً في الأخرى منها (فهما أمران ( متغايران ) قطعاً لامتناع ( اجتماع المتنافيين في ذات واحدة (ضعيف لجواز أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف في العضو ) لضعف الآلة واختلاف (فيها لا لضعف وقوة في ذات القوة. ( ثم) نقول في إبطال القوى لا سيما القوة المصورة كما زعموه إن ( من تأمل في عجائب الأفعال الحادثة في علم الطبيعة ) من النباتات المتخالفة الأنواع والحيوانات المتباينة الحقائق ( البالغة ) تلك الأفعال العجيبة ) من الإتقان والإحكام ( أقصى الغاية وكان ذلك التأمل ) راجعاً إلى فطنة وإنصاف باقياً على فطرة الله تعالى التي فطر الناس عليها ) من الذكاء والميل إلى الصواب لم يعم بصيرته التقليد ) من أهل الأهواء ( ولم يكن
And what is said in explaining the multiplicity of faculties: 'We see the organ strong in one of them, i.e., one of the faculties, and weak in another of them; so they are two things distinct without doubt, because it is impossible for two contradictory things to coexist in a single essence'—this is weak, because it is possible that the difference in the organ is due to weakness of the instrument and difference in it, not due to weakness and strength in the essence of the faculty. Then, we say in refuting the faculties, especially the formative faculty as they claim: 'Indeed, whoever contemplates the wonders of actions occurring in the science of nature—plants of diverse species and animals of varying natures, those wonderful actions reaching the utmost degree of perfection and precision, and that contemplation is based on intelligence and fairness, remaining upon the fitra of God Most High with which He created people, of intelligence and inclination to correctness—his insight will not be blinded by imitation from the people of passions, and he will not be'...
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, it refutes an argument for multiple faculties based on observation that an organ can be strong in one function and weak in another, which supposedly proves distinct faculties. The author says this is weak because the difference could be due to the condition of the instrument (e.g., the muscle or nerve), not the strength of the faculty itself. Second, the author calls for refuting the multiplicity of faculties, especially the formative faculty (القوة المصورة), by contemplating the wonders of natural actions. Such contemplation, if done with intelligence and fairness, prevents one from being blinded by blind imitation of those following passions. The text ends abruptly but clearly advocates that true understanding comes from direct reflection on nature's perfection, not from following philosophical doctrines.

Key Terms
القوة
الآلة
اجتماع المتنافيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That strength in one function and weakness in another must prove two different faculties.
Correction: The difference may be due to the condition of the instrument, not a difference in the essence of the faculty.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the argument for multiple faculties based on strength/weakness weak?
A) Because strength and weakness are not real.
B) Because the difference could be due to the instrument, not the faculty's essence.
C) Because multiple faculties cannot exist in one organ.
D) Because the observation is false.

Key Takeaway
Observing strength and weakness in functions does not necessarily prove distinct faculties; the instrument's condition must be considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you notice strengths and weaknesses in your own spiritual life—such as being diligent in prayer but careless with charity—do you tend to view them as separate 'faculties' of your soul, or do you consider the condition of your heart (the instrument)? How might this lesson shift your approach to self-improvement?
?
Think of a recent situation where you judged someone's character based on an apparent inconsistency. Could the 'instrument' (their upbringing, environment, or health) explain the difference rather than a defect in their essence? How does this lesson call you to be more charitable in your assessments?
?
If the same faculty can produce different results depending on its instrument, what does that mean for your own efforts to strengthen your faith? Are you focusing on the right 'instrument'—the habits and contexts—rather than merely wishing for a stronger 'faculty'?

Reflecting on Nature to Avoid Blind Imitation
Sanusi urges contemplation of nature to avoid blind imitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how contemplation of natural wonders, guided by fitra, protects one from blind imitation of false doctrines.

Core Idea
Contemplating nature with a sound fitra frees the mind from blind imitation.

Having refuted the philosophical arguments for multiple faculties, the author now prescribes a positive method: direct reflection on the wonders of nature. By observing the perfect design of plants and animals with intelligence and fairness, one can remain on the natural disposition (fitra) and avoid being misled by the imitation of those who follow their passions. This lesson explores that call.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to see how the author transitions to this positive exhortation. Notice the phrase 'من تأمل في عجائب الأفعال'.
2
Identify the key elements of the contemplation
The passage mentions: contemplator, objects of contemplation (plants, animals), results (perfection, precision), and prerequisites (intelligence, fairness, fitra). List them.
The goal is to prevent blindness from imitation (تقليد).
3
Understand the role of fitra
The author says 'فطرة الله التي فطر الناس عليها' – fitra of God upon which He created people. Note how fitra here means natural intelligence and inclination to truth.
Fitra is a safeguard against false doctrines.
4
Connect to the broader argument
This passage is the capstone: instead of trusting philosophical arguments, the author urges direct observation of nature. This is a methodological shift from dialectic to empirical reflection.
The text ends abruptly, but the message is clear.
