The Halo: Formation, Visibility, and Weather Signs
Describes the formation of a halo, its visibility, and weather indications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the optical mechanism behind the lunar and solar halo and how its appearance predicts weather changes.

Core Idea
The halo is a reflection, not a physical ring.

This passage from the classical text explains the halo around the moon and sun. It describes why we see a ring of light around the moon, not a direct image, and how its behavior forecasts weather. The author also notes the rarity of the solar halo and a case of a rainbow-colored halo.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the descriptive style with examples from Ibn Sina.
2
Understand the invisibility of the cloud part facing the moon
The author explains that the part of the cloud directly between the moon and the observer is invisible because the moon's brightness overwhelms it. Ask yourself: why does the rest of the cloud appear as a ring?
This is due to reflection of light.
3
Learn the three weather indications
The passage gives three signs: tearing from all sides (fair weather), thickening until vanishing (rain), and inclining to one side (wind). Memorize these.
These are practical predictions based on empirical observation.
4
Note the special cases: double halos and solar halo rarity
The author mentions double halos when two cloud layers exist, and the rarity of the solar halo. Also note the colored halos observed by Ibn Sina.
The solar halo is rare because sunlight breaks up thin clouds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه رأى سبع نور ضعيف وتسمى (الهالة) وإنما لا يرى الجزء الذي يقابل القمر من ذلك الغيم، لأن قوة الشعاع تخفي حجم السحاب الذي لا يستره فلا يرى فيه خيال القمر، كيف والشيء إنما يرى على الاستقامة نفسه لا شبحه بخلاف أجزائه التي لا تقابله فإنها تؤدي خيال ضوئه كما عرفت قبل وأكثر ما تتولد الهالة عند عدم الريح فإن تمزقت من جميع الجهات دلت على الصحو وإن ثخن السحاب حتى بطلت دلت على المطر لأن الأجزاء المائية قد كثرت وإن انحرفت من جهة دلت على ريح تأتي من الجهة . وإذا اتفق أن توجد سحابتان على الصفة المذكورة إحداهما تحت الأخرى حدثت هناك هالة تحت هالة وتكون التحتانية أعظم لأنها أقرب إلينا. واعلم أن هالة الشمس وتسمى الطفاوة بضم الطاء نادرة جدا، لأن الشمس تحلل السحب الرقيقة ومع ذلك فقد زعم ابن سينا أنه رأى حول الشمس هالة تامة في ألوان قوس قزح ورأى بعد ذلك هالة فيها قوسية قليلة، وإنما تنفرج هالة الشمس إذ كثف السحاب وأظلم .. وحكى أيضاً أنه رأى حول القمر هالة قوسية اللون لأن السحاب كان غليظاً فتقوس في أجزاء الضوء وعرض ما يعرض للقوس
That he saw a weak light called the halo. The part of that cloud which faces the moon is not seen because the strength of the ray hides the body of the cloud which does not screen it, so the moon's image is not seen in it. How? For a thing is only seen directly, not its reflection, unlike those parts which do not face it, for they convey the reflection of its light as you learned before. Most often the halo is generated when there is no wind. If it tears from all sides it indicates fair weather; if the cloud thickens until it vanishes, it indicates rain because the watery particles have increased; if it inclines from one side, it indicates wind coming from that side. If it happens that two clouds exist in the described manner, one under the other, a halo occurs under a halo, and the lower one is larger because it is nearer to us. Know that the halo of the sun, called al-ṭufāwah (with ḍamma on ṭā'), is very rare, because the sun dissolves thin clouds. Nevertheless, Ibn Sīnā claimed that he saw around the sun a complete halo in the colors of the rainbow, and after that a halo containing a slight arch. The halo of the sun only appears when the cloud is thick and dark... He also narrated that he saw around the moon a halo with the color of the rainbow because the cloud was thick, so it arched in the parts of light, and there occurred what occurs for the rainbow.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage explains the phenomenon of the lunar halo (الهالة). The author begins by noting that the part of the cloud directly in front of the moon is not seen because the moon's brightness overwhelms it, while the surrounding cloud reflects the light and appears as a ring. The halo's behavior predicts weather: tearing from all sides indicates fair weather, thickening until disappearance indicates rain, and inclination indicates wind direction. If two cloud layers exist, two concentric halos appear, with the lower one larger. The sun's halo (الطفاوة) is rarer because sunlight dissolves thin clouds; Ibn Sīnā is cited as having observed one with rainbow colors. A moon halo with rainbow colors can also occur when the cloud is thick, causing refraction similar to the rainbow.

Key Terms
الهالة
الطفاوة
انعكاس
السحاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The halo is a physical ring around the moon or sun.
Correction: It is an optical phenomenon created by reflection and refraction of light in thin clouds.

Checkpoint

A) Rain
B) Fair weather
C) Wind
D) Snow

Key Takeaway
The halo is an optical effect of reflected light, and its changes in shape indicate weather patterns.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The halo is seen indirectly because the direct ray of the moon is too strong to perceive the cloud in front. In your spiritual life, where might your own 'strong light'—pride, ambition, or comfort—be hiding a truth you need to see? What 'cloud' are you ignoring because its visibility is blocked by your own intensity?
?

If your spiritual state were a halo, what would its 'tearing,' 'thickening,' or 'tilting' tell you about the coming 'weather' of your heart? Examine this honestly: Are you seeing signs of drought or downpour in your relationship with Allah?

Defending the Majority View on Halo and Rainbow
A scholar rejects the author's explanation; the author invokes majority opinion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author transmits the majority explanation for the halo and rainbow despite a scholar's objection.

Core Idea
The author follows the majority, not his own certainty.

In this passage, the author reports that a respected scholar of optics rejects the explanation for the halo and rainbow that the author has presented. Rather than engaging in detailed refutation, the author simply states that his account is the opinion of the majority, and he is transmitting it in deference to them. This reveals the author's methodological humility and respect for scholarly consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the description of the scholar and the author's justification.
The passage is short but reveals the author's methodology.
2
Identify the scholar and his claim
The scholar is al-Ḥasan al-Fārisī, an expert in optics. He claims the author's explanation is false.
The author does not name the scholar's own theory; only records the rejection.
3
Reflect on the author's response
The author does not defend his view with new arguments. Instead, he invokes the authority of the majority opinion (ra'y al-jumhūr).
This shows the author's commitment to transmitting consensus even when challenged.

ورأيت بعض فضلاء زماننا ممن له في علم المناظر كعب عال وهو المولى الفاضل كمال الملة والدين الحسن الفارسي الله برد مضجعه يدعي بطلان ذلك الذي ذكرناه من أسباب الهالة وقوس قزح لكنه أي ما ذكرناه فيها رأي الجمهور قد ذكرناه متابعة لهم
And I saw some of the virtuosos of our time, one who has a high standing in the science of optics, al-Mawlā al-Fāḍil Kamāl al-Milla wa al-Dīn al-Ḥasan al-Fārisī (may God cool his resting place), claims the falsity of what we have mentioned of the causes of the halo and rainbow. But it—that is, what we mentioned about them—is the opinion of the majority; we have mentioned it following them.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This short passage reports that a distinguished scholar, al-Ḥasan al-Fārisī, an expert in optics, rejects the author's explanation of the halo and rainbow. The author responds that his account is simply the majority view (رأي الجمهور), and he is transmitting it in deference to the consensus, not necessarily endorsing it as absolute truth. This reveals the author's methodological commitment to presenting received knowledge while acknowledging alternative opinions.

Misconception: That the author is claiming his explanation is indisputable.
Correction: He is merely reporting the majority view, not claiming absolute truth.

Why does the author present the majority opinion even if a scholar rejects it?
A) He has no other theory
B) He is following the majority
C) He thinks the scholar is wrong
D) He wants to refute the scholar

Key Takeaway
The author transmits the majority view on the halo and rainbow out of deference to consensus, not personal conviction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief simply because it was the majority view, without personal conviction? How does the author's example challenge you to examine your own adherence to teachings?
?
When someone disagrees with a position you hold, do you defend it as absolute truth or acknowledge that you may be following others? What would it take for you to honestly say 'I follow the majority'?
?
The author humbly transmits the majority view without claiming certainty. Where in your life do you need to adopt this humility about your own knowledge, especially in matters of religion?

Method of Learning

The Causation of Accidents: Correlation and Suspension
Al-Iji presents and evaluates two views on causation of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correlation argument (dawaran) is insufficient to prove causality, and why al-Iji suspends judgment on the cause of a substance's acceptance of accidents.

Core Idea
Correlation alone does not prove causation; both views are possible.

This short note (tanbih) addresses a key methodological question in kalam: Can we deduce causality from coextensiveness? Some theologians argue that a substance's ability to accept accidents is caused by its occupancy of space, because they always occur together. Others point out that the correlation is symmetrical, so it does not favor one direction over the other. Al-Iji examines both arguments and concludes that without decisive proof, the intellectually honest stance is to suspend judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a counter-claim, and a conclusion.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the correlation argument
Identify the first view: acceptance is caused by occupancy because they co-vary. Grasp the logical principle of correlation (dawaran) as a method for establishing causality.
The argument uses the presence/absence test: if A and B always coexist, A is the cause of B.
3
Understand the objection
The second view rejects this because the correlation is symmetrical. Both occupancy and acceptance always appear together, so neither can be singled out as the cause without additional evidence.
This is a classic critique of inferring causation from correlation.
4
Grasp the conclusion of suspension
Al-Iji chooses tawaqquf (suspension) because both positions are rationally possible and there is no decisive proof. Recognize that this is a principled epistemic stance, not agnosticism.
In kalam, tawaqquf is a legitimate position when evidence is balanced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : منهم أي من المتكلمين من قال قبول الأعراض الثابت للجواهر معلل بالتحيز للدوران فإنه إذا وجد التحيز وجد القبول، وإذا عدم عدم والمدار علة للدائر : وقيل : لا لدوران كل منهما مع الآخر فليس إسناد أحدهما إلى الآخر أولى من العكس والحق التوقف لأن كل واحد من المذهبين ممكن ولا قاطع في شيء منهما.
A note: Among them — that is, among the theologians — some said that the acceptance of accidents which is established for substances is caused by occupancy (al-tahayyuz), because of correlation (al-dawaran): for when occupancy exists, acceptance exists; and when it is absent, acceptance is absent. And the coextensive term is the cause of the coextensive term. And it was said: No, because of the correlation of each of them with the other, so assigning one to the other is not more warranted than the reverse. The truth is suspension, because each of the two views is possible and there is no decisive proof for either of them.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This note (تنبيه) addresses the question of why substances (جواهر) have the property of accepting accidents. Some theologians say that this ability is caused by the substance's occupancy of space (التحيز). They argue by correlation (الدوران): whenever there is occupancy, there is acceptance; whenever acceptance is absent, occupancy is absent. This coextensiveness suggests a causal relationship. Others disagree, arguing that the correlation is reciprocal, so it is not clear which is the cause. The author (المصنف) concludes that the correct position is to suspend judgment (التوقف), because both views are logically possible and there is no decisive evidence for either.

Key Terms
قبول الأعراض
التحيز
الدوران
التوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that constant correlation (dawaran) is sufficient to prove causation in kalam.
Correction: This passage shows that symmetry of correlation prevents a definitive causal attribution. The author suspends judgment precisely because correlation alone is not decisive.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Iji suspend judgment on whether occupancy causes acceptance of accidents?
A) Because he denies any causal relationship in the physical world.
B) Because the correlation is reciprocal and there is no decisive proof.
C) Because the theologians who argued for causation were all wrong.
D) Because revelation explicitly forbids such inquiries.

Key Takeaway
When evidence is mutually balanced and inconclusive, the rational method is to suspend judgment rather than make an unfounded causal claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly about your faith or life. Are you sure it is based on decisive evidence, or could it be a conclusion you jumped to because of a perceived correlation? How would al-Iji's principle of tawaqquf challenge you to re-examine that belief today?
?
Recall a time when you were pulled between two possible explanations for something in your religious understanding. Did you feel pressure to choose one, or were you comfortable suspending judgment? What would change if you consistently applied the lesson's call to withhold certainty until clear proof is given?
?
This passage shows that even careful scholars sometimes cannot decide. Are there areas in your own life where you claim certainty without enough evidence—perhaps about others' intentions or about theological details? What would it cost you to stop there and say 'I do not know' in obedience to the method of tawaqquf?

Introduction to the Third Observatory on Souls
Al-Jurjani announces the Third Observatory on souls and lists four maqasid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani structures his work by transitioning from the study of bodies to the study of immaterial souls, and be aware of the four main sections that follow.

Core Idea
The study of souls begins where the study of bodies ends.

This short passage marks a major turning point in the Mawaqif. After a lengthy treatment of bodies and their accidental properties, al-Jurjani now announces the Third Observatory, dedicated to immaterial souls. He also gives a preview: the discussion will be organized under four central themes, or maqasid. Let's read the passage and then see what these four objectives are.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its literal translation. Notice how the author signals a shift in topic.
The word 'marsad' (observatory) is a metaphor for a major section.
2
Identify the three key elements
The passage contains: (1) a subject announcement (souls), (2) a transition note (after bodies), and (3) a structural outline (four maqasid).
3
Understand the methodological significance
Ask yourself: Why would the author explicitly mark this transition? This shows that the Mawaqif is carefully organized, and each section builds on the previous one.
4
Look ahead to the four maqasid
The next lessons will detail each of the four maqasid. This lesson sets the stage for that detailed discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث في مباحث النفوس المجردة وأحكامها شرع في بيانها بعد الفراغ من مباحث الأجسام وعوارضها ( وفيه مقاصد ) أربعة .
The Third Observatory: On the studies of immaterial souls and their rulings. He began explaining them after finishing the studies of bodies and their accidents. And it contains four maqasid (objectives).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a methodological transition. After completing the study of bodies (ajsam) and their accidental properties (awarid), the author now turns to immaterial souls (nufus mujarrada) and their rulings (ahkam). He states that this observatory (marsad) contains four maqasid (objectives or sections). The passage serves as a heading and outline, indicating that the following material is organized around these four themes. This structure is typical of the encyclopedic approach of Sharh al-Mawaqif.

Key Terms
نفوس مجردة
أحكام
أجسام
عوارض
مقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does this passage indicate follows the study of bodies?
A) The study of celestial spheres
B) The study of immaterial souls
C) The study of divine attributes
D) The study of bodily accidents

Key Takeaway
The Third Observatory marks a shift from bodies to souls and is divided into four main sections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have now moved from studying external bodies to internal souls. Are you truly ready to turn inward? What have you neglected about your own soul that you need to examine today? Commit to one concrete step to begin this inner study.
?
The author announces this shift after 'finishing' the study of bodies. Have you ever 'finished' with the material world to focus on your soul? What keeps you attached to physical concerns, and how will you detach yourself to pursue this deeper knowledge?
?
This observatory contains 'four maqasid' or objectives. What are your personal objectives for studying your soul? Will you approach this with the same seriousness as you do worldly knowledge, or will you let it remain abstract?
